**Summary.** The paper demonstrates that topological phases can protect local quantum information from the scrambling effects of many-body dynamics. By utilizing Majorana zero modes, the authors show that a nonzero boundary Quantum Fisher Information can be maintained for exponentially long times. This effect is characterized by a distinct asymmetry in encoding channels that is robust to both disorder and interactions.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant for many-body dynamics and quantum information as it proposes a way to use topological protection to preserve metrological sensitivity, offering a robust protocol for local information encoding and readout in noisy environments.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** In many-body systems, unitary dynamics typically cause locally encoded information to delocalize and vanish. The authors seek to demonstrate how topological phases can prevent this dispersal to provide a form of topological metrological memory.

**Main result.** The authors show that in the topological phase of a Kitaev chain, a Majorana zero mode pins the boundary Quantum Fisher Information (QFI) to a nonzero plateau that persists for exponentially long times. They identify a unique boundary encoding-axis asymmetry as a robust diagnostic for this topological protection.

**Method.** The study uses an analytical approach via Bogoliubov-de Gennes formalism and Jordan-Wigner transformation, complemented by real-time numerical simulations of finite-size chains to test robustness against disorder and interactions.

**Model / system.** An anisotropic XY spin-1/2 chain with open boundaries, which maps to a 1D Kitaev chain (topological superconductor) under the Jordan-Wigner transformation.

**Key observables.** Local Quantum Fisher Information (QFI), boundary-axis asymmetry (comparing R_y and R_x encoding channels), and the Majorana localization length.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Transverse field (h), anisotropy (gamma), system size (L), Majorana localization length (xi), and the energy scales of the bulk gap and Majorana splitting.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The protocol assumes a specific time-averaging window to isolate stationary signals from dephasing; the readout is assumed to be local at the boundary to avoid the non-locality of the Jordan-Wigner string.

**Figures summary.** The notes do not provide specific figure descriptions, though they mention the presence of figures illustrating the boundary QFI plateau and the asymmetry between encoding channels.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of information delocalization, defines the XY/Kitaev model, provides analytical derivations for the QFI and its topological plateau, presents numerical evidence for robustness against disorder and interactions, and concludes with scaling analysis near the phase transition.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

In many-body quantum systems, unitary dynamics generically delocalize locally encoded information, causing single-site metrological sensitivity to vanish. We analytically demonstrate that a topological phase can prevent this dispersal. In the open Kitaev chain, a Majorana zero mode fixes the boundary quantum Fisher information (QFI) at a nonzero plateau that persists for times exponentially long in system size. We derive exact analytical expressions for the local QFI and identify the mechanism as the spatial separation of the two Majorana quadratures to opposite ends of the chain. This separation produces a boundary encoding-axis asymmetry that distinguishes topological boundary memory from a generic localized subgap signal. We show numerically that the asymmetry is robust to moderate quenched on-site disorder, while the boundary plateau remains visible under parity-preserving interactions in finite-size real-time simulations. The protocol requires only product-state initialization, Hamiltonian evolution, and single-site readout.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.00571"></a>
### [Quantum corrections to the Josephson dynamics: a population-imbalance approach](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00571v1)

**Authors:** Oliver Hideg, Sofia Salvatore, Luca Salasnich  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum gases · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.00571v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **4/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00571_figures/2605.00571_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. The exact evolution (solid, blue) of ⟨ˆz(t)⟩and ⟨ˆϕ(t)⟩, the mean-field solution (dotted, red) and the quantum effec- tive mean-field (dashed, orange) for Λ = 1 and N = 40. Initial conditions where set to z0 = 0 and ϕ0 = 0.1. The quantum effective dynamics is more accurate than the mean-field.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00571_figures/2605.00571_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> FIG. 2. The exact evolution (solid, blue) of ⟨ˆz(t)⟩and ⟨ˆϕ(t)⟩ and the quantum effective mean-field (dashed, orange) for Λ = 40 and N = 40. The initial conditions where set to z0 = 0 and ϕ0 = 0. The mean-field is not shown as it almost com- pletey overlaps with the quantum effective mean-field. Instad, we show a combination: the mean-field elliptic cosine with the only-z corrected frequency (32) (dash-dot, green). The hybrid approach benefits from both the quantum correction and the elliptic correction to the time period (44). However, neither can account for the amplitude modulation caused by higher energy levels influencing the dynamics in the fully quantum case.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00571_figures/2605.00571_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> FIG. 3. The four plots show the predicted Josephson oscilla- tion frequency for Λ = 1, 10, 40, 80 together with the energy gap between the ground state and first excited state of the Hamiltonian obtained numerically as a function of the num- ber of bosons N. To be able to compare them, the vertical axis shows energy in units of 2J and frequency in units of 2J/ℏ. The imbalance only prediction (dashed) is closer to the numerical (dots) than the phase only prediction (dash-dot) for Λ ≲80. The mean-field (solid), that is independent of N, is accurate only as N →∞.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper develops a theoretical method to calculate quantum corrections to the dynamics of two coupled Bose-Einstein condensates by focusing solely on population imbalance. It demonstrates that this approach is superior to the standard phase-only method when interactions are strong. The results include explicit expressions for corrected potentials and frequencies that match exact numerical results.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a new theoretical framework for describing many-body dynamics in the strong interaction regime, offering a more robust alternative to standard phase-only approximations in bosonic Josephson junctions.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating quantum corrections to the Josephson dynamics of two weakly coupled Bose-Einstein condensates using a population-imbalance-only approach.

**Main result.** The population-imbalance approach provides a more accurate description of quantum-corrected Josephson frequency in the strong interaction regime compared to the traditional phase-only approach.

**Method.** The authors use a one-loop quantum effective action and a covariant background-field method to derive an effective potential and mass, validated via exact diagonalization of the Bose-Hubbard model.

**Model / system.** A two-site Bose-Hubbard model representing a bosonic Josephson junction consisting of two weakly coupled Bose-Einstein condensates.

**Key observables.** Effective potential V_eff(z), effective mass m_eff(z), quantum-corrected Josephson frequency Omega_J, fractional imbalance, and phase difference.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Dimensionless interaction parameter Lambda = UN/2J, total number of bosons N, tunneling energy J, and interaction strength U.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes weakly coupled condensates and small-amplitude oscillations around the Josephson fixed point.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 compares the exact, mean-field, and quantum effective mean-field evolution of the fractional imbalance and phase difference.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins with the derivation of a population-imbalance-only Lagrangian from a two-variable action, proceeds to compute one-loop quantum corrections using the background-field method, and concludes with numerical validation against exact diagonalization.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate quantum corrections to the Josephson dynamics of two weakly coupled Bose-Einstein condensates using the population imbalance as the sole dynamical variable. Starting from the two-variable action, we derive the imbalance-only Lagrangian with a position-dependent mass and quantize it via symmetric operator ordering. The leading quantum corrections to the classical potential and mass are computed via the one-loop quantum effective action, using a covariant background-field method that fully accounts for the coordinate dependence of the mass. This yields explicit expressions for the effective potential and the effective mass, from which we derive the quantum-corrected Josephson frequency. Numerical comparison with exact diagonalization of the two-site Bose-Hubbard model shows that the imbalance-only formulation outperforms the complementary phase-only approach in the regime of strong interactions, which is the natural domain of validity of the population-imbalance description.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.00679"></a>
### [Entropy transport through a superfluid quantum point contact: A Keldysh field-theory approach](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00679v1)

**Authors:** Davide Bertolusso, C. J. Bolech, Thierry Giamarchi  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum gases · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.00679v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **4/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5**

**Summary.** This paper uses Keldysh field theory to study how particles and entropy flow through a superfluid quantum point contact. It demonstrates that the non-monotonic, oscillatory behavior of the entropy current is a direct consequence of Multiple Andreev Reflection processes. The work highlights the necessity of including particle-hole asymmetry to accurately model heat and entropy transport.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides deep insight into non-equilibrium thermodynamics and transport in many-body systems, specifically how microscopic pairing mechanisms (MAR) manifest as macroscopic oscillatory transport properties in superfluid/superconducting junctions.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the mechanisms of particle and entropy transport through a superfluid quantum point contact, specifically focusing on the origin of oscillatory behavior in the entropy current.

**Main result.** The authors find that entropy current oscillations are driven by the activation of successive Multiple Andreev Reflection (MAR) processes rather than the divergence of the local density of states, and that quadratic dispersion is essential to capture heat transport.

**Method.** A Keldysh field-theory approach is used to compute out-of-equilibrium steady-state currents, utilizing a Nambu-spinor representation and frequency-space analysis.

**Model / system.** The system consists of two ultra-cold neutral Fermi-gas reservoirs in a superfluid state connected by a quantum point contact, modeled using BCS mean-field theory and a tunneling Hamiltonian with quadratic dispersion.

**Key observables.** Particle current (JN), heat current (JH), and entropy current (JS).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Chemical potential difference (delta-mu), temperature difference (delta-T), superconducting gap (delta), and junction transparency (T).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The reservoirs are assumed to be in separate local equilibria, and the study focuses on the DC component of the currents, averaging out AC components.

**Figures summary.** Schematics of the junction and Andreev processes (Fig 1, 2, 3, 10); comparison of LDoS for linear vs. quadratic dispersion (Fig 5); current behavior in different junction types (Fig 6); and the persistence of oscillations in a truncated model (Fig 12).

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the transport problem, describes the Keldysh formalism and Hamiltonian, analyzes the role of dispersion and Andreev reflections, presents results for SS and SN junctions, and compares findings with experimental cold-atom data.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study the matter and entropy transport between two ultra-cold neutral Fermi-gas reservoirs linked by a quantum point contact under a chemical-potential gradient. We describe the two leads with a BCS mean-field model and derive the current-bias characteristics for both particle and entropy transport. We compute the out of equilibrium steady-state currents by using the Keldysh formalism. In accordance with previous works in the literature, we confirm the well-known behavior for the particle current and extend the computation to the entropy current in the BCS regime. The entropy current shows an oscillatory behavior at low voltage in the ballistic junction limit. We analyze the results for a wide range of values of the junction's transparency. We also compare our findings with experimental results in cold atomic gases in the unitary regime.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.00772"></a>
### [Entanglement capacity of complex networks from quantum walks](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00772v1)

**Authors:** Pravy Prerana, Sascha Wald  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.00772v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

Each node n is split into a source and target (n±) and states |n →m⟩are mapped to |n+⟩⊗|m−⟩. The bold red edges form a maximum matching of B(G) and correspond to the DTQW state illustrated in panel (a). (c) Source-target entanglement generated during the time evolu- tion of a DTQW on networks with different structural prop- erties. Low clustering, hub dominated structures yield higher...</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00772_figures/2605.00772_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">

We consider the three-regular symmetric digraph in panel (a) and generate Haar random states in the arc ba- sis. In panel (b) we evaluate the source-target entanglement of ten states (colored cross) and compare it to the coin-walker entanglement for all (3!)6 = 46656 distinct coin assignments (scatter lines, black crosses indicating averages).</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00772_figures/2605.00772_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> FIG. 3. Source–target entanglement in random graphs. Panels (a) and (b) show the time evolution of the source-target entanglement entropy Sst for ER and BA graphs respectively. Faint lines depict the entanglement entropy for 100 different graph realizations and the solid line is the average of these realizations. We observe that DTQWs on both network models exhibit higher entanglement at low connectivity, whereas increasing connectivity reduces the entanglement. Panels (c) and (d) present scatter plots of the time-averaged plateau values from panels (a) and (b) against the average clustering coefficient C of each realization, for the ER and BA models, respectively. We again observe that...</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** The paper proposes a new entanglement measure, 'source-target entanglement,' that works for quantum walks on irregular networks where standard methods fail. It reveals that the maximum entanglement a network can support is limited by its graph-theoretic matching size and that higher connectivity or clustering can actually hinder entanglement generation.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a new way to characterize quantum correlations in complex topologies, which is highly relevant for understanding how quantum information spreads in non-regular quantum networks and many-body systems with complex connectivity.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Quantifying entanglement in discrete-time quantum walks on irregular or complex networks where traditional coin-walker entanglement is not well-defined due to varying node degrees.

**Main result.** The authors introduce 'source-target entanglement' and prove that the entanglement capacity of a graph is upper-bounded by the logarithm of the size of the largest matching in its bipartite double cover, finding that increased connectivity and clustering actually suppress entanglement.

**Method.** The study uses a bipartite double cover construction to define a new entanglement measure and employs graph theory (matching theory) alongside numerical simulations on Erdős–Rényi and Barabási–Albert random graphs.

**Model / system.** Discrete-time quantum walks (DTQWs) on complex networks, specifically using symmetric digraphs, Erdős–Rény_en (ER) graphs, and Barabási–Albert (BA) graphs with Grover coin dynamics.

**Key observables.** Source-target entanglement (S_st), coin-walker entanglement, and graph matching size.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Network connectivity (p for ER, m for BA), clustering coefficient, and time evolution steps.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study relies on the bipartite double cover as a theoretical proxy for the coin-walker division and focuses on symmetric digraphs.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the bipartite double cover construction and the impact of clustering; Figure 2 compares different coin assignments; Figure 3 shows the time evolution of entanglement and its anti-correlation with the clustering coefficient in random graphs.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces a new entanglement measure for irregular graphs, establishes a theoretical upper bound based on graph matchings, demonstrates the tightness of this bound, and then explores how network topology (connectivity and clustering) affects entanglement dynamics in random graph models.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Discrete-time quantum walks provide a natural framework for quantum transport on complex networks. On regular structures, coin-walker entanglement has been widely used to characterize quantum transport and to support quantum algorithmic protocols. However, this notion relies on a fixed Hilbert space factorization separating coin and position and is therefore not directly applicable to more complex, irregular structures. Here we introduce an entanglement measure for general networks based on a bipartition that assigns each node two roles, acting as both a source and a target. The resulting bipartition defines the source-target entanglement, a measure for general networks, motivated by coin-walker entanglement. We show that the connectivity of the network imposes an upper bound on this entanglement and identify graph matchings as the underlying structure governing entanglement generation. We further illustrate that in random graphs improving graph connectivity reduces the attainable entanglement, establishing a structure-dependent constraint on quantum correlations.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Entanglement Distance of Two- and Multi-Qubit Variational States and Its Quantification with Quantum Computing](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00259v1)

**Authors:** Kh. P. Gnatenko, R. O. Hredil, V. Y. Pinchuk, M. Z. Seniak, Yu. T. Shevchuk  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.00259v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper provides an analytical framework to calculate the entanglement distance in variational quantum circuits. It proves that as the circuit depth increases, the influence of neighboring qubits spreads, effectively increasing the entanglement-dependent neighborhood. The theoretical results are highly consistent with numerical simulations of quantum circuits.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is relevant for understanding the dynamics of information spreading and entanglement growth in parameterized circuits, which is fundamental to the study of many-body dynamics and the performance of variational quantum algorithms.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to quantify the 'entanglement distance' and characterize the spreading of quantum correlations in variational quantum circuits as circuit depth increases.

**Main result.** The authors derived analytical recurrence relations for Pauli expectation values and demonstrated that increasing circuit depth expands the neighborhood of qubits that influence a target qubit's local observables.

**Method.** The researchers used the Heisenberg picture to evolve Pauli operators backward through the circuit and applied the Clifford property of CZ gates to derive exact analytical expressions, which were then verified via Qiskit numerical simulations.

**Model / system.** The model consists of two-qubit and multi-qubit systems arranged in a closed one-dimensional chain with periodic boundary conditions, utilizing a layered variational ansatz of RY rotations and CZ entangling gates.

**Key observables.** Entanglement Distance (EED), Pauli expectation values (X, Y, Z), and two-qubit correlations.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Circuit depth (number of layers), rotation angles (theta), and the distinction between local and global rotation parameters.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The system starts in the |00...0> state, and the circuit transformations are strictly real, implying the Y-component of the Bloch vector is zero.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the circuit architecture; Figures 2-4 present surface plots of EED for two qubits; Figure 5 shows a 5-qubit chain circuit; Figures 6-7 show entanglement distance and correlation landscapes as functions of rotation angles.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the entanglement distance concept, details the two-qubit analytical derivation using the Heisenberg picture, extends the analysis to a 1D chain to show correlation spreading, and concludes with numerical validation and comparison of local vs. global rotation effects.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study the entanglement distance of variational quantum states for two-qubit and multi-qubit systems. These states are constructed using variational quantum circuits with $R_Y$ rotations and entangling $CZ$ gates.For the two-qubit case, we analytically derive recurrence relations for expectation values of Pauli observables using. This approach allows us to analytically calculate quantum correlators and evaluate the entanglement distance depending on the circuit parameters and depth. The analysis were extended to a closed one-dimensional chain of $N$ qubits. It is shown that with increasing circuit depth, more qubits influence a given qubit, which reflects the spreading of quantum correlations in the system. For a closed one-dimensional chain of $N$ qubits, explicit analytical expressions are derived for the case of two layers. The results are compared with numerical simulations performed using quantum programming tools. The results agree with the theoretical predictions.

</details>

### [Exploring the Geometric and Dynamical Properties of Spin Systems and Their Interplay with Quantum Entanglement](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00241v1)

**Authors:** Jamal Elfakir

**Summary.** This thesis investigates how the geometry of quantum state space, characterized by metrics and curvature, relates to the dynamical evolution and entanglement of spin systems. It demonstrates that quantum correlations effectively 'compress' the state space by reducing its curvature and geometric phase. The work bridges classical symplectic mechanics with quantum information geometry.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a unified geometric framework for understanding how entanglement modifies the topology and curvature of state space, which is fundamental for developing robust geometric quantum gates and understanding many-body dynamics.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The research explores the interplay between the geometric/topological properties of quantum state space and the dynamical evolution of spin systems, specifically regarding entanglement and evolution speed.

**Main result.** The study establishes that increasing entanglement decreases the curvature of the state space and reduces the accumulated geometric phase, with higher spin values enhancing these variations.

**Method.** The author utilizes the mathematical framework of projective Hilbert space, applying the Fubini-Study metric, symplectic geometry, and the Gauss-Bonnet theorem to analyze quantum states.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on spin systems, including two-body anisotropic Heisenberg (XXZ) models and many-body all-range Ising models for both spin-1/2 and general spin-s particles.

**Key observables.** Quantum entanglement (concurrence, I-concurrence), geometric phase (Berry, Aharonov-Anandan), evolution speed, and G-curvature.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Interaction anisotropy, coupling intensity, spin value (s), number of particles (N), and the thermodynamic/large-spin limits.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis is primarily restricted to unitary evolution in closed quantum systems.

**Figures summary.** Plots illustrating the relationship between evolution speed, entanglement, and Fubini-Study distance, as well as curvature and geometric phase as functions of I-concurrence.

**Paper structure.** The thesis progresses from classical Hamiltonian mechanics and symplectic structures to the geometric description of quantum states, concluding with the application of these tools to analyze the dynamics and topology of Heisenberg and Ising spin models.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

This thesis, explores the quantum entanglement and evolution through both a geometric and dynamical perspective. The first part focuses on classical phase space and its central role in Hamiltonian mechanics, emphasizing the importance of symplectic structures in describing mechanical states. The study highlights the formal analogy between classical phase space and the Hilbert space used in quantum mechanics. The second part is devoted to the geometric description of quantum states through the projective structure of Hilbert space. Emphasis is placed on the geometric interpretation of quantum evolution, particularly via the Fubini-Study metric, associated symplectic structures, and the geometric phase acquired during unitary evolutions. The final two parts are dedicated to the study of spin systems (both two-body and many-body) under different interaction models (XXZ Heisenberg and all-range Ising). Both the dynamical aspects (evolution speed, entanglement, and the quantum brachistochrone problem) and the geometric and topological structures of the corresponding quantum states are analyzed.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.00287"></a>
### [Sequential Measurements as a Resource for Quantum Metrology](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00287v1)

**Authors:** Koray Mentesoglu, Rahul Trivedi, Sara Mouradian  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.00287v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `quantum measurements` **4/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00287_figures/2605.00287_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. a A QHO feels a force f(t), which manifests as a displacement in phase space. A single interrogation time consists of N signal interactions. b Single Measurement Protocol: Phase is accumulated in the QHO to gain a factor of N advantage in precision. The signal displacement ˆD(β) is followed by an ancilla-assisted measurement (orange box) which includes a state-dependent coupling displacement and projective measurement on the qubit. c Sequential Measurement Protocol: The sequential-measurement protocol repeats the coupling and measurement operations at every round. The qubit is reset into the ground state, |↓⟩, before the next round, but QHO evolution continues.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00287_figures/2605.00287_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> FIG. 3. QFI comparison at N = 100 and |α| = 0.2. In light blue, single-parameter estimation using N measurements. In blue, single-parameter estimation using 2N measurements. In dashed dark blue, two-parameter estimation using 2N mea- surements plotting only FQ 11. The single-parameter curves share a similar shape, while the two-parameter curve in- cludes an increased range of optimal |α|. Inset: Though 2N measurements for both protocols yield comparable informa- tion gain for FQ 11, the plotted two-parameter protocol also has information about the other parameter FQ 22, whereas the single-parameter protocol only gives information about a sin- gle quadrature.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00287_figures/2605.00287_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> FIG. 2. a QFI vs. |α| for N ∈{410, 450, 500} for the single- (orange) and sequential- (blue) measurement protocols. The sequential-measurement protocol exhibits larger information gain and a wider range of optimal |α|. Increasing N will in- crease the overall FQ gain. b Heat map of scaling exponent, p = d log FQ/d log N, for different measurement strengths and N. An example contour, p = 2.25, is plotted. c Scal- ing exponent p plotted as a function of N|α|2 for increasing N. The weak and strong measurement regimes are defined by N|α|2 ≪1 and N|α|2 ≫1.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00287_figures/2605.00287_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> FIG. 4. **Summary.** This paper proposes a protocol using sequential weak measurements to simultaneously estimate both quadratures of a quantum displacement. It demonstrates that this approach can achieve enhanced scaling of information, increase dynamic range, and remain robust against environmental decoherence.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a novel way to use measurement backaction as a resource and demonstrates how sequentiality can fundamentally change the scaling of precision and robustness to noise in open quantum systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing a quantum metrology protocol for the simultaneous estimation of both quadratures of a displacement channel while overcoming limitations like decoherence and phase wrapping.

**Main result.** Sequential weak measurements act as a resource that can increase information gain via measurement backaction, achieve N^3 scaling of Quantum Fisher Information in certain regimes, and provide robustness to decoherence.

**Method.** The authors use a sequential measurement protocol on an ancilla qubit coupled to a harmonic oscillator, employing a Dicke basis to reduce computational complexity and analyzing information via Quantum and Classical Fisher Information.

**Model / system.** A Quantum Harmonic Oscillator (QHO) coupled to an ancilla qubit, subject to a displacement channel and subject to environmental decoherence (loss and dephasing) modeled by a Lindblad master equation.

**Key observables.** Measurement outcomes of the ancilla qubit (N-bit string), Hamming weights, and Quantum/Classical Fisher Information.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Displacement parameters (beta_1, beta_2), measurement strength (alpha), number of measurement rounds (N), and effective decay rate (Gamma).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The thermal occupancy of the oscillator is assumed to be negligible (n_th approx 0), and the protocol assumes the QHO cannot be measured directly.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 illustrates the single vs. sequential measurement protocols; Fig 2 shows QFI superiority and scaling exponents; Fig 3 compares single vs. two-parameter performance; Fig 4 shows CFI dips; Fig 7 shows the monotonic decrease of CRB in the presence of decoherence; Fig 8 compares joint-system QFI.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the sequential protocol, establishes the mathematical framework using the Dicke basis, compares scaling regimes (weak vs. strong measurement), extends the protocol to two-parameter estimation, analyzes robustness against decoherence, and discusses connections to grid states.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We present a protocol in which sequential weak measurements of a quantum harmonic oscillator enable simultaneous estimation of both quadratures of a displacement channel. Calculations of the quantum Fisher information show that the measurement backaction can increase the information gained for a range of measurement strengths. The protocol distributes information over a $N$-bit string after $N$ weak measurements. Thus we find that post-processing can be used to avoid information loss due to phase wrapping, increasing the effective dynamic range. Finally, the periodic information extraction makes the protocol robust to decoherence. Our results establish mid-sensing measurement as a resource for single- and multi-parameter quantum metrology.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.00104"></a>
### [Criticality on Rényi defects at (2+1)$d$ O(3) quantum critical points](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00104v1)

**Authors:** Yanzhang Zhu, Zhe Wang, Meng Cheng, Zheng Yan  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.00104v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00104_figures/2605.00104_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. Schematic illustration of the columnar dimerized Heisenberg model and entanglement bipartitions. Thick blue bonds represent the strong couplings J′, and thin black bonds denote the weak couplings J. The dashed line indicates the entanglement bipartition, which separates the system into subsystem A (in black) and its environment ¯A (in white). Panels (a) and (b) correspond to ordinary and special cuts, while panel (c) corresponds to the extraordinary cut realized by a tilted cut in the same model. These three cuts are used throughout the paper to realize the three classes of Rényi de- fects compared below.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00104_figures/2605.00104_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> FIG. 2. Finite-size scaling of observables for ordinary and spe- cial Rényi defects at n = 2 in the columnar dimerized Heisen- berg model. Left panels: ordinary; right panels: special. Top row shows the spin correlation Cs(L) on the Rényi line defects, and bottom row shows the Binder cumulant U2(L). System sizes range from L = 16 to 80. Solid lines are fits described in the text.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00104_figures/2605.00104_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> FIG. 3. Finite-size scaling of observables for the extraordinary Rényi defect at different Rényi indices. Columns (left to right) correspond to n = 2, n = 4, and n = 10. Top row shows the spin correlation Cs(L) on the Rényi defect line, and bottom row shows the Binder cumulant U2(L). For n = 4 and n = 10, we additionally show the squared magnetization M 2 z . System sizes range from L = 16 to 80. Solid lines are fits described in the text.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00104_figures/2605.00104_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> FIG. 4. Binder cumulant U2 as a function of Rényi index n for (a) ordinary, (b) special, and (c) extraordinary Rényi defects in the columnar dimerized Heisenberg model. Differ- ent symbols correspond to system sizes L = 16, 24, 32. For the ordinary and special cuts, U2 evolves smoothly with n and no crossing is observed, consistent with the absence of a finite-n phase transition. In contrast, for the extraordinary cut the Binder cumulant curves cross near nc ≈3–4, indicat- ing a transition between a disordered regime at small n and an ordered regime at larger n.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that the universality of Renyi entanglement entropy is determined by the specific geometry of the entanglement cut, which can lead to different defect universality classes. It identifies a phase transition on the 'extraordinary' defect line driven by the Renyi index. This provides a framework to understand why entanglement scaling can appear sensitive to microscopic lattice details.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates why the universal scaling of Renyi entanglement entropy depends on the microscopic details of the entanglement cut and explores the universality classes of codimension-two Renyi defects at (2+1)d O(3) quantum critical points.

**Main result.** The authors find that different entanglement cuts realize distinct Renyi defect universality classes (ordinary, special, and extraordinary) and provide evidence for a phase transition on the extraordinary defect as a function of the Renyi index n.

**Method.** Large-scale quantum Monte Carlo simulations on n-sheeted replica manifolds are used to measure local observables and extract scaling dimensions via finite-size scaling analysis.

**Model / system.** The study uses (2+1)d quantum spin models at the O(3) Wilson-Fisher quantum critical point, specifically the 1/2 columnar dimerized Heisenberg model and the spin-1/2 bilayer Heisenberg model.

**Key observables.** Renyi-n expectation values, defect spin-spin correlation functions, and the defect Binder cumulant.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Renyi index n, system size L, and the coupling ratio J'/J used to reach the critical point.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Numerical access is restricted to positive integer n > 1 via the replica approach; the behavior of the special defect remains somewhat inconclusive due to subtle numerical signals.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows schematic entanglement cuts (ordinary, special, extraordinary); Figure 2 shows finite-size scaling for ordinary/special cuts; Figure 3 shows scaling for the extraordinary defect; Figure 4 shows the Binder cumulant crossing for the extraordinary cut.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of entanglement cut sensitivity, describes the quantum spin models and replica QMC method, presents results for different defect types (ordinary, special, extraordinary), discusses the phase transition in the extraordinary case, and concludes with implications for entanglement entropy scaling.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

At a quantum critical point, the universal scaling behavior of Rényi entanglement entropy is controlled by the universality class of the codimension-two Rényi (or conical) defects in the infrared theory. In this work we perform a systematic study of critical correlations along Rényi defect lines in (2+1)d quantum spin models realizing quantum phase transitions described by the O(3) Wilson-Fisher universality class, using large-scale quantum Monte Carlo simulations. We present numerical evidence that, for a fixed Rényi index $n$, there exist multiple Rényi defect universality classes, with distinct critical exponents for the O(3) order parameter on the defect. These universality classes are realized by choosing microscopically different entanglement cuts in lattice models, which we classify as ordinary, special and extraordinary according to their relation to surface criticality. For the extraordinary entanglement cut, we further find evidence for a phase transition on the defect as a function of the Rényi index. Our results highlight the key role of defect universality classes in determining the universal scaling of Rényi entropy, and provide a framework for understanding the previously observed dependence of Rényi entropy scaling on microscopic lattice details.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.00521"></a>
### [Induced discommensurations in the lock-in transition of charge-density waves](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00521v1)

**Authors:** Katsuhiko Inagaki, Satoshi Tanda

**Summary.** This paper uses simulated annealing to study the lock-in transition in charge-density waves. It finds that discommensurations are topologically protected and can drive a commensurate system into an incommensurate state, providing a theoretical basis for observed current-induced incommensuration in materials like o-TaS3.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Understanding the mechanism behind the emergence of discommensurations during the lock-in transition of charge-density waves (CDWs) and explaining current-induced incommensuration.

**Main result.** The study demonstrates that discommensurations are topologically protected and can be excited in the commensurate state, driving the system toward an incommensurate state, which is consistent with experimental observations in o-TaS3.

**Method.** Numerical optimization using simulated annealing based on the Metropolis algorithm to minimize the McMillan free energy functional.

**Model / system.** The model uses McMillan's free energy functional for charge-density waves in quasi-one-dimensional conductors like o-TaS3 and K0.3MoO3.

**Key observables.** CDW intensity, wave profiles, phase jumps, and free energy relative to the commensurate state.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The parameter Y (representing the third-order Umklapp process), the critical value Yc, and the phase theta.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The model assumes a second-order transition via the McMillan free energy, though the authors note that the Umklapp term might induce a first-order transition.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the evolution of wave profiles during annealing; Figure 2 shows wave profiles for various Y values; Figure 3 shows profiles for different initial slopes; Figure 4 compares free energies of discommensurations and soliton-antisoliton pairs.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins with a validation of the numerical method, followed by an analysis of wave profiles near the critical value, an investigation of discommensurations in the commensurate state, a discussion of topological protection and energy asymmetry, and finally a comparison with experimental results.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We studied McMillan's free energy of the lock-in transition in charge-density waves. The wave profiles near the critical value were obtained by numerical annealing. First, we demonstrated that our method reproduces the previous studies. The obtained wave profiles include discommensurations near the critical value. Then, we calculated possible wave profiles in the commensurate state. We found that discommensurations are able to be excited in the commensurate state, leading the system to turn out to an incommensurate state. We proposed that these wave profiles result from topological invariants. Moreover, excitation of the discommensurations is favorable for the direction to the original wavelength of the incommensurate state. This is attributed to the nature of McMillan's free energy. The current-induced incommensurations, which we discovered with the diffraction study of $o$-TaS$_3$ [Inagaki \textit{et al.}, J. Phys. Soc. Jpn. 77, 093708 (2008)], is consistent with this study.

</details>

### [Probability Distribution Analysis of the Cascaded Variational Quantum Eigensolver](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00807v1)

**Authors:** Yi-Hua Lai, John P. T. Stenger, Gloria Bazargan, Igor V. Schweigert, Daniel Gunlycke  
**Type:** both · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.00807v1>

**Summary.** This paper investigates the Cascaded Variational Quantum Eigensolver (CVQE), an algorithm designed to reduce communication between quantum and classical units. By analyzing probability distributions, the authors show how to optimize guiding-state parameters to maintain chemical accuracy and maximize signal-to-noise ratios on noisy quantum hardware.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides practical insights into error mitigation and parameter tuning for VQE-like algorithms on NISQ devices, specifically showing how to balance adiabaticity and noise susceptibility.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Optimizing the selection of guiding states in the Cascaded Variational Quantum Eigensolver (CVQE) to achieve accurate many-electron ground-state solutions with minimal resource consumption and robustness against hardware noise.

**Main result.** The study demonstrates that CVQE can achieve chemical accuracy even with single-step adiabatic preparation and that tuning the adiabatic energy scale (h-Omega) can maximize the signal-to-noise ratio on real NISQ hardware.

**Method.** The authors use a trapezoidal-state preparation method for the CVQE algorithm, comparing noiseless simulations with IBM quantum hardware (ibm_aachen) and utilizing classical benchmarks like CCSD and FCI.

**Model / system.** A prototypical bimolecular reaction (H2 + H2+ -> H3+ + H) modeled using a many-electron Hamiltonian and a 256-dimensional Fock space, mapped to 8 qubits via the Jordan-Wigner transformation.

**Key observables.** Ground-state energies (Eg), reaction energies (Delta E), and probability distributions of the quantum state at various algorithm stages.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Discretization steps (K), adiabatic energy scale (h-Omega), sampling scale (shot counts), and the adiabaticity condition (h-Omega K^-1 << h-omega_0 << h-Omega).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The use of a minimal STO-6G basis set and the assumption that the classical optimization can compensate for distortions in the guiding state if the sampled Fock state set is representative.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows energy evolution via discretization; Figure 2 plots the minimal-energy path; Figure 3 shows molecular structures; Figure 8 shows noiseless probability distributions; Figure 9 and 10 illustrate how the guiding-state distribution and sampling counts change with the adiabatic energy scale on real hardware.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the CVQE algorithm, defines the trapezoidal-state preparation method, analyzes different parameter regimes (adiabaticity and sampling) on simulators, evaluates performance on real IBM hardware, and provides a symmetry-based justification for the Fock state distributions in the appendix.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The cascaded variational quantum eigensolver (CVQE) circumvents the need for iterative communication between the quantum and classical processing units that is necessary in the conventional VQE algorithm. While CVQE offers complete freedom to choose the guiding state as input, not all guiding states suffice for solution accuracy, as well as resource efficiency. Our work presents a process based on trapezoidal-state preparation for selecting guiding states that yield accurate many-electron ground-state solutions with minimal resource consumption. By analyzing the state probability distributions at different stages of the CVQE calculations, we determine the optimal guiding-state parameters for given resource constraints. We demonstrate the process by comparing electronic energies along the minimal-energy path for a prototypical bimolecular reaction, $\mathrm{H}_2 + \mathrm{H}_2^+ \rightarrow \mathrm{H}_3^+ + \mathrm{H}$, using Noisy Intermediate-Scale Quantum (NISQ) computing.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.00118"></a>
### [Toward Secure Multitenant Quantum Computing: Circuit Affinity, Crosstalk Patterns, and Grouping Strategies](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00118v1)

**Authors:** Andrew Woods, Chi-Ren Shyu  
**Type:** both · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.00118v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `QC/QI experiment` **4/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00118_figures/2605.00118_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Fig. 1: Visual overview of the experimental methodology. Stage 1 executes spatial partitioning and padding to generate the mapping set M. Stage 2 utilizes a verification pipeline (Solo vs Cotenant) to isolate crosstalk from temporal drift.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00118_figures/2605.00118_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Fig. 4: This chart illustrates how similarly each of the ma- chines behave. A higher score means that a scheduler cali- brated for one machine can be deployed for another machine. The sequence of lower scores for ibm_miami (Nighthawk r1) indicates a recalibration for complete revisions of new circuits will be needed.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00118_figures/2605.00118_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> Fig. 2: Flow of adding more and more stress on QFT circuit multitenancy with the strong to weak interference circuits. The weak to strong interference add the circuits in reverse order starting with adding QAOA and ending with adding GA.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00118_figures/2605.00118_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> Fig. 3: Overall interaction scores between circuits across all machines. All noise levels have been shifted so that the stan- dalone baseline is 0. The columns show an uneven structure indicating that circuits are not simply ”noisy” but convey a complex relationship.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00118_figures/2605.00118_fig5.jpg" alt="Figure 5">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 5.</b> Fig. 5: A higher level overview of circuit types. These values are averaged amount the SSIM scores between each processor type. We see that there is a noticeable drop in the score between the Nighthawk r1 and Heron r1 processors where the score is approximately 0, indicating a need for a complete scheduler recalibration across generations. However, only a slight recalibration would be needed for processors of the same type.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper investigates how running multiple quantum circuits simultaneously on the same hardware causes crosstalk that can degrade performance and compromise security. By characterizing which circuits are 'aggressive' or 'sensitive,' the authors propose a framework for a scheduler to pair compatible jobs to maximize system utility and stability.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study addresses the security and performance risks introduced by inter-circuit crosstalk during multitenant execution in cloud-based quantum computing, specifically focusing on how concurrent jobs can lead to fault-injection or information leakage.

**Main result.** The authors identify three classes of circuits (universally aggressive, universally sensitive, and cotenant-dependent) and demonstrate that while crosstalk patterns are transferable within hardware revisions, they are fundamentally different across different topologies like Heavy-Hex and Square lattices.

**Method.** The researchers used a 'Sandwiched Baseline-Cotenant Calibration' strategy to isolate crosstalk from temporal drift and applied the Structural Similarity Index (SSIM) to quantify the consistency of interference patterns across seven IBM superconducting processors.

**Model / system.** The experimental platform consists of seven IBM superconducting quantum processors, including various generations of the Heron (Heavy-Hex lattice) and Nighthawk (Square lattice) architectures.

**Key observables.** Crosstalk Aggression (N), Crosstalk Sensitivity (S), Structural Similarity Index (SSIM), t-statistic of interference, and Bhattacharyya Coefficient for fidelity.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Padding depth (p) for spatial isolation, circuit depth, 2-qubit gate ratio, and architectural topology (Heavy-Hex vs. Square).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study uses a pairwise approximation for circuit interactions, which may not fully capture the complexity of much higher-load multitenant scenarios.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the experimental methodology and mapping flow; Figure 2 illustrates cumulative stress-tests with increasing circuit load; Figure 3 displays interaction scores across different machines; Figure 4 and 5 show SSIM scores indicating hardware transferability and architectural differences.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of multitenancy security, describes the hardware and circuit selection, details the experimental protocols for isolation and mapping, presents the classification of circuits and architectural similarity results, and concludes with implications for hardware-aware scheduling.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Multitenancy increases throughput and reduces costs in cloud-based quantum computing, but concurrent job execution introduces security risks through inter-circuit crosstalk. We characterize the structural predictability of these interference patterns across seven IBM superconducting processors, spanning Heron (r1-r3) and Nighthawk (r1) architectures and five different circuit types. We evaluate pairwise interactions, by applying the Structural Similarity Index (SSIM) and a structural $t$-statistic to the concurrent execution of five foundational quantum circuits (QAOA, Grover's, QPE, QFT, and ZZFeatureMap), we quantify behavioral consistency across disparate hardware. Our results identify three types of circuits: universally aggressive, universally sensitive, and cotenant-dependent circuits. Aggressive circuits, such as Grover's Algorithm, exhibit a statistically significant interference pattern, yielding a $t$-statistic range of $[1.37,2.61]$ relative to the standalone baselines across all tested pairings. Conversely, sensitive circuits, such as the Quantum Fourier Transform, demonstrate a disproportionate susceptibility to multitenant execution, showing high deviations from single-tenant computational behavior. We demonstrate that crosstalk signatures are highly consistent within architectural revisions--with intra-revision similarity reaching $0.77$ (Hr3) and $0.68$ (Hr2)--while inter-revision similarity drops to $0.43$. Furthermore, we identify a ``topological decoupling" between Heavy-Hex and square lattice systems, where structural similarity falls to $0.01$ between Heron r1 and Nighthawk r1. These findings provide an empirical foundation for hardware-aware schedulers to strategically pair jobs, maximizing system utilization while preserving computational integrity.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

**Summary.** This paper proposes a new type of atomic interferometry using spin-orbit-coupled spin-1 Bose gases. By tuning the Raman coupling, researchers can independently control spin interactions to create entanglement and use spatial density patterns as a new way to read out phase information. This approach allows for precision measurements that can surpass the standard quantum limit.

**Why it may be interesting.** It presents a highly controllable platform for nonlinear atom interferometry, offering a unique way to implement time-reversal (echo) protocols and exploit spin-momentum locking for new types of quantum-enhanced sensing.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing a flexible quantum interferometry scheme that overcomes the limitations of conventional spinor BECs, specifically the difficulty in independently controlling spin-mixing interactions and measurement strategies.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that Raman-dressed spin-1 gases allow for enhanced metrological sensitivity beyond the standard quantum limit using both ground-state and excited-state quantum phase transitions, and provide a novel readout via spatial density modulations.

**Method.** The study uses a three-mode truncation of the effective Hamiltonian, exact diagonalization, the Truncated Wigner Approximation (TWA) for quantum dynamics, and Gross-Pitaevskii Equation (GPE) simulations.

**Model / system.** A Raman-dressed spin-1 Bose-Einstein condensate with synthetic spin-orbit coupling, described by an effective spinor Hamiltonian where the spin-mixing interaction strength can be tuned via Raman coupling.

**Key observables.** Quantum Fisher Information (QFI), estimation precision, magnetization, quadrupolar operator, and spatial density modulations (density stripes).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Raman coupling (Omega), linear and quadratic detuning (delta, epsilon), spin-mixing interaction strength (lambda), and the P, TF, and BA ground-state phases.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The low-energy dynamics are assumed to be localized around the three minima of the dispersion branches (three-mode truncation), and TWA accuracy is noted to be limited to short time scales.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the Raman coupling scheme, dispersion bands, and ground-state phase diagram; Fig 2 illustrates the sequential protocol; Fig 3 displays energy spectra, ESQPT lines, and QFI dynamics; Fig 4 demonstrates phase extraction via density stripe shifts.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by introducing the Raman-dressed platform and its effective Hamiltonian, moves to describing specific interferometric protocols (sequential and echo-type), analyzes metrological enhancement via ground-state and excited-state criticality, and concludes with a novel spatial readout strategy.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We propose and analyze a quantum interferometry scheme based on a Raman-dressed Bose gas with spin-orbit coupling. In this system, the atom-light coupling mixes spin and momentum degrees of freedom, giving rise, in the low-energy regime, to an effective spinor condensate whose spin-mixing interaction can be tuned independently of the atomic density. This controllability enables a separation between state preparation and phase imprinting, and provides a natural route to echo-type protocols based on effective time reversal. Within this framework, critical regimes of the effective spinor Hamiltonian can be used to generate entanglement and enhance interferometric sensitivity beyond the standard quantum limit. In addition, the spin-momentum locking of the dressed modes gives access to spatial density modulations that provide an alternative readout of the interferometric phase. In particular, phase information can be extracted from the displacement of spin-orbit-induced density stripes even when conventional spin observables are insensitive within the effective spinor description. Our results identify Raman-dressed spinor gases as a flexible platform for nonlinear atomic interferometry, combining controllable spin-mixing dynamics with spatially resolved phase readout.

</details>

**Summary.** The paper proposes a new all-optical saddle trap for levitated nanoparticles using structured light to reduce decoherence. This platform allows for advanced quantum tasks such as controlled wavefunction expansion and highly sensitive force detection in the zepto-Newton range.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a novel platform for studying open quantum systems by demonstrating how structured light can be engineered to suppress specific decoherence channels like photon recoil.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The need for levitated optomechanical platforms that minimize decoherence from photon recoil and thermal radiation while enabling controlled quantum experiments like delocalization and force sensing.

**Main result.** An all-optical rotating saddle trap using structured light (Gaussian and Laguerre-Gauss modes) suppresses photon recoil decoherence and enables force detection sensitivity in the zepto-Newton regime.

**Method.** The study uses a master equation approach to model decoherence and a Quantum Fisher Information Matrix analysis to derive fundamental limits for force metrology.

**Model / system.** A mesoscopic, levitated nanoparticle (radius 50 nm) in a rotating, time-dependent quadratic potential created by a superposition of Gaussian and Laguerre-Gauss optical modes.

**Key observables.** State purity, logarithmic negativity (entanglement), momentum squeezing, and minimum detectable force.

**Important parameters / regimes.** LG mode intensity fraction (IL), rotation frequency (Omega), gas-limited coherence time (Tgas), and numerical aperture (NA).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The system is treated within the dipole limit and quadratic approximation, assuming the particle motion remains within a characteristic length scale L.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows purity evolution; Figure 2 illustrates particle delocalization and recovery protocols; Figure 3 plots entanglement vs. rotation frequency; Supplemental Figure S1 details trap stability and depth.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the saddle trap architecture, analyzes the stability and validity of the quadratic approximation, quantifies decoherence suppression and entanglement generation, and concludes with a metrological application for force detection.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate the quantum dynamics of a levitated nanoparticle in a structured light rotating saddle-like optical potential consisting of a superposition of Gaussian and Laguerre-Gauss modes with detuned frequencies. This rotating saddle trap offers unique opportunities for quantum experiments, such as reduced decoherence due to photon recoil and absorption, the possibility of large delocalization of the particle's center-of-mass motion, particle recovery protocols, the generation of motional entanglement and momentum squeezing. As an application, we show that this saddle-trap architecture enables force detection with sensitivity in the zepto-Newton regime.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.00117"></a>
### [Topological Charge of Causality at a PT-Symmetric Exceptional Point](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00117v1)

**Authors:** Kejun Liu  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.00117v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `driven-dissipative phase transition` **3/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **2/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00117_figures/2605.00117_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00117_figures/2605.00117_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">

</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that causality in PT-symmetric systems is not just a binary property but carries a topological charge that changes at an exceptional point. It shows that while standard Kramers-Kronig relations fail in the broken phase, a corrected version can be derived. The study also reveals that the violation of standard KK relations is most intense at the phase transition threshold.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a novel topological interpretation of causality in non-Hermitian systems and introduces a model-independent diagnostic for identifying non-Hermitian gain through the breakdown of standard dispersion relations.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating whether the failure of standard Kramers-Kronig relations in non-Hermitian systems can be described as a topological transition where causality carries a topological charge.

**Main result.** The authors show that causality in a PT-symmetric dimer carries a topological charge (Blaschke winding number) that jumps from 0 to 1 at the exceptional point, and that the violation of standard KK relations scales with a negative exponent of approximately -1.08.

**Method.** Analytical derivation using Heisenberg-Langevin equations, input-output relations, and the Blaschke winding number framework, supplemented by numerical verification of the residue-corrected dispersion relation.

**Model / system.** A PT-symmetric open dimer coupled to a one-port waveguide, modeled by an effective non-Hermitian Hamiltonian with gain-loss parameter gamma and coupling kappa.

**Key observables.** Blaschke winding number (Nb), KK residual (Delta_KK), and the scaling exponent (nu).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Gain-loss strength (gamma), coupling strength (kappa), and external coupling/decay (gamma_ex).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The system is treated within the Markov approximation using the Gardiner-Collett interaction; the scaling results are derived for a single-port geometry.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the trajectory of the reflection coefficient pole in the complex plane and a phase diagram of the exceptional point; Figure 2 compares phase diagrams for different coupling geometries.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of KK violation, defines the PT-symmetric dimer model, derives the topological charge via the Blaschke winding number, presents the residue-corrected KK relation, analyzes the scaling of the KK residual, and proposes an experimental protocol.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Causality in linear response is conventionally treated as a binary property: a response function is either analytic in the upper half-plane or it is not. We show that in a PT-symmetric open dimer it instead carries a topological charge. As the gain-loss parameter crosses the exceptional point, a single pole of the reflection coefficient migrates into the upper half-plane, the Blaschke winding number jumps from 0 to 1, and standard Kramers-Kronig (KK) reconstruction acquires a Lorentzian residual fixed by the pole residue. The transition is sharp, protected by the codimension-one structure of the exceptional point, and directly measurable in a one-port reflection experiment. Most strikingly, the violation magnitude scales as Delta_KK ~ |gamma - gamma_c|^nu with nu ~ -1.08 in the single-port geometry: the breakdown of standard KK is strongest at threshold and weakens deeper in the broken phase. We derive the exact reflection coefficient, verify the residue-corrected dispersion relation, and propose a THz time-domain spectroscopy protocol that detects the topological charge through the residual itself.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.00656"></a>
### [Renormalized entropy production for optimal transport in jump processes: Make conservative forces optimal again](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00656v1)

**Authors:** Andreas Dechant, Jann van der Meer  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.00656v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5**

This requires us to transport probability from site 1 to site 3, which can be done...</figcaption>

**Summary.** The paper identifies that standard entropy production is not minimized by conservative forces in jump processes. By proposing a 'renormalized' version of entropy production, the authors restore the principle that conservative forces are the optimal drivers for a given time evolution. This provides a more consistent thermodynamic framework for studying transport in discrete systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to open quantum systems and many-body dynamics as it provides a new framework for understanding optimal driving and dissipation in discrete-state stochastic dynamics, which is essential for controlling quantum master equations.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** In discrete-state Markov jump processes, the conservative forces that drive a system do not minimize the standard entropy production, breaking the fundamental connection found in continuous diffusion processes.

**Main result.** The authors introduce 'renormalized entropy production,' a new quantity that is uniquely minimized by conservative forces, thereby restoring the link between conservative driving and optimal transport.

**Method.** The study uses optimal transport theory, variational calculus with Lagrange multipliers, and Kullback-Leibler divergence analysis of trajectory probabilities.

**Model / system.** The model consists of Markov jump processes on a discrete state space governed by a Master Equation, where transition rates are parameterized by symmetric (activity) and skew-symmetric (thermodynamic force) components.

**Key observables.** Entropy production rate, renormalized entropy production, thermodynamic forces, probability currents, and dynamical activity.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Near-equilibrium vs. far-from-equilibrium regimes, inverse temperature (beta), and mobility (mu).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The state space is connected, the process is Markovian, and microscopic reversibility (detailed balance) holds for the underlying transition structure.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates a transport problem on an 8-site ring with a weak link; Figure 2 shows the time-evolution of occupation probabilities for optimal and near-optimal protocols; Figure 3 compares probability currents in bulk vs. link paths.

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the discrepancy in jump processes, constructs the renormalized entropy production, provides numerical illustrations, explores trajectory-dependent counterparts, and discusses speed limits and path-probability interpretations.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

For continuous-space diffusion processes, there is a strong connection between conservative forces and entropy production. For a given time evolution of the system's state, the entropy production is minimized when the system is driven by a unique conservative force. However, this relation does not extend to jump processes on a discrete state space. In this case, the forces that minimize the entropy production are generally nonconservative, this effect is more pronounced far from equilibrium in the presence of high energy barriers. Here we show that, while conservative forces do not minimize the entropy production for a given time evolution, they are nevertheless uniquely characterized as the minimizer of a quantity we dub the renormalized entropy production. This work explores the properties this quantity shares with entropy production as well as crucial differences between them. We also discuss the conceptual and physical differences between the corresponding optimization problems in finite time. Our theoretical calculations are illustrated with explicit numerical examples.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<img src="2605.00446_figures/2605.00446_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> FIG. 4. Storage efficiency ηs in (a) and (b), plotted for the stable regime with geff &gt; δ and geff &lt; δ, respectively. For (a) and (b), the horizontal axis is the dimensionless ratios µ/Γeff and geff/δ, respectively. For the panel (b), since in this case δ ≫Γeff, the performance is dictated by the ratio of geff to δ. The curves correspond to cooperativities C = |Gge|2N/(γκ) = 1, 3, 10. The fixed parameters are the same as those in Fig. 2. The horizontal dotted line in both panels marks ηret = 1, above which the total output exceeds the ideal single-photon limit and indicates amplification.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00446_figures/2605.00446_fig5.jpg" alt="Figure 5">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 5.</b> FIG. 5. Storage efficiency for the threshold and unstable regimes, plotted as a function of the dimensionless total pro- tocol time ΓeffT. The threshold regime satisfies Γeff = µ, and the unstable regime satisfies Γeff &lt; µ. The curves correspond to cooperativities C = |Gge|2N/(γκ) = 1, 3, 10 in each regime. The fixed parameters are the same as those in Fig. 3 with δ = 1 MHz. The horizontal dotted line marks ηtot = 1, above which the total output exceeds the ideal single-photon limit and indicates amplification. In these two cases, the memory effectively operates as a photon source, emitting photons con- tinuously over time.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper studies how extra energy levels and unwanted field couplings impact the performance of cavity-based quantum memories. It reveals that while these couplings can boost efficiency through amplification, they simultaneously degrade the fidelity of the stored quantum state. The study establishes a clear distinction between functional memory operation and unwanted photon amplification.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a practical framework for optimizing realistic quantum memories by moving beyond idealized Lambda models, specifically addressing how noise and gain channels break time-reversal symmetry and affect fidelity.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating how unwanted couplings and additional energy levels in a four-level system affect the performance of cavity-based ensemble quantum memories, specifically distinguishing between faithful storage and unwanted amplification.

**Main result.** The authors identify three dynamical regimes (stable, threshold, and unstable) and demonstrate an efficiency-fidelity trade-off where unwanted couplings can increase total efficiency (even leading to amplification) at the cost of reduced fidelity.

**Method.** A fully quantum treatment using Heisenberg-Langevin equations, involving adiabatic elimination of excited states and the cavity mode, and a Bogoliubov transform to solve the dynamics.

**Model / system.** A four-level extension of a cavity-based Lambda-type ensemble memory involving a ground state, a metastable spin state, and two excited states, subject to both desired and off-resonant unwanted couplings.

**Key observables.** Single-photon storage efficiency, retrieval efficiency, operational fidelity (signal-to-noise ratio), and photon flux.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Cooperativity (C), effective decay rate (Gamma_eff), effective coupling (g_eff), ground-state splitting (delta), and the ratio mu/Gamma_eff.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Low-excitation/weak-excitation regime, adiabatic elimination of excited states and cavity mode, bad-cavity approximation, and neglecting spin decoherence.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the four-level energy scheme; Figure 2 shows retrieval efficiency vs. mu/Gamma_eff; Figures 4 and 7 plot storage efficiency and fidelity across different regimes and cooperativity values; Figure 8 compares efficiency in threshold and unstable regimes over time.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the four-level model, derives the effective dynamics and analytical expressions for efficiency and fidelity, identifies the three dynamical regimes, analyzes the efficiency-fidelity trade-off, and concludes with implications for realistic warm-vapor memories.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum-memory models often reduce complex level structures to an idealized $Λ$ system, potentially missing nearby levels and unwanted couplings that can qualitatively alter the predicted performance. Here, we study an extension of a cavity-based $Λ$-type ensemble memory, a four-level model with unwanted couplings from both the control field and signal, using a fully quantum treatment. We derive explicit expressions for the single-photon storage efficiency, retrieval efficiency, and fidelity, and on this basis identify three distinct dynamical regimes: stable, threshold, and unstable. Within the stable regime, we additionally discriminate between two qualitatively different sub-regimes. Applying the theory to warm-vapor-inspired parameters, we determine the conditions under which the system can still operate as a high-quality quantum memory. More generally, our results provide a practical framework for distinguishing genuine memory operation from amplification and for optimizing realistic quantum memories beyond idealized models.

</details>
