<a id="paper-2605.00286"></a>
### [Unified approach to time-resolved x-ray and electron diffraction imaging](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00286v1)

**Authors:** Mingrui Yuan, Nikolay V. Golubev  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.00286v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00286_figures/2605.00286_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. Laser-driven electron dynamics in graphene. Top panel: The blue curve represents the vector potential of the pump laser field polarized along x direction, while the red line depicts the evolution of the population of the conduction band of graphene. Bottom panel: The snapshots of the integrated real space electron difference density (contour plots) and the electron current density (arrows). The four columns depict the time instances tI = 9.8 fs, tII = 11.2 fs, tIII = 11.8 fs, and tIV = 20.0 fs, indicated by the vertical grey dashed lines in top panel. More detailed discussion of the utilized computational procedure can be found in Ref. [99].</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00286_figures/2605.00286_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> FIG. 2. (b) and (c) Comparison of density-density (orange solid line), density-current (purple dashed line), and current-current (green dash-dotted line) contributions to the time-resolved diffraction signal at the [1, 1] and [1, −1] Bragg spots of graphene, respectively. The inset schematically shows the positions of the graphene Bragg spots and highlights those analyzed in panels (b) and (c).</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper presents a unified theoretical framework for both time-resolved x-ray and electron diffraction imaging using quantum field theory. By establishing a common formalism, it allows for a consistent comparison of how different probes capture ultrafast quantum dynamics. The authors demonstrate the utility of this approach by simulating laser-driven electron motion in graphene.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a rigorous field-theoretic bridge between different types of ultrafast probes, which is highly relevant for researchers studying the coherent interaction of light and matter and the development of new quantum imaging techniques.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The lack of a unified theoretical framework to describe and compare time-resolved x-ray diffraction (TR-XRD) and ultrafast electron diffraction (TR-UED) within a consistent formalism.

**Main result.** The authors develop a unified quantum-field-based description that encompasses both TR-XRD and TR-UED, allowing for the systematic disentanglement of their similarities and differences.

**Method.** The approach uses quantum field theory and time-dependent perturbation theory to derive scattering probabilities, extending the time-dependent formulation of x-ray scattering to arbitrary particles.

**Model / system.** The model describes the interaction between a probe beam (photons for x-rays or electrons for TR-UED) and a target system, specifically applied to laser-driven electron dynamics in monolayer graphene.

**Key observables.** Scattering probability, differential scattering cross-section, electron density dynamics, and electron current dynamics.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Ultrafast/non-equilibrium regimes, femtosecond and sub-femtosecond temporal resolution, and the relativistic Breit interaction for electron scattering.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes the unperturbed Hamiltonian is separable, neglects electron exchange between beam and target, and uses the non-retardation approximation for the electromagnetic interaction.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by identifying the need for a unified framework, establishes a general quantum-field-based scattering theory, derives specific matrix elements for both x-ray and electron beams, and concludes with a numerical simulation of electron dynamics in graphene.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Time-resolved x-ray diffraction (TR-XRD) and ultrafast electron diffraction (TR-UED) are emerging tools for probing ultrafast quantum dynamics. From a theoretical perspective, they are commonly described within different frameworks and modeled using distinct approximations. Here, we present a unified quantum-field-based description of ultrafast diffraction imaging that permits consistent consideration of TR-XRD and TR-UED within a common theoretical formalism. Our approach elucidates the correspondence between TR-XRD and TR-UED and allows their similarities and differences to be systematically disentangled. The developed formalism is sufficiently general to consistently and straightforwardly incorporate additional physical effects of interest, such as relativistic charge-current and current-current couplings. We apply our approach to simulate diffraction measurements of laser-driven electron dynamics in graphene, demonstrating the unique capabilities of diffraction imaging to unravel intricate quantum processes in matter.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.00132"></a>
### [Entanglement Enabled Data Transmission over an Arbitrarily Varying Channel](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00132v1)

**Authors:** Janis Nötzel, Florian Seitz  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.00132v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **3/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00132_figures/2605.00132_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Fig. 1. Depicted are O and ∆as part of the convex polytope P({0, 1}2), using r = A = 2. We observe that O approaches q0 and q1 in its limits.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** The paper shows that entanglement can be used to establish shared correlations between a sender and receiver to overcome jamming attacks in optical channels. By using Two-Mode Squeezed Vacuum states, the parties can transform a zero-capacity, symmetrizable channel into a functional communication channel. This provides a way to 'bootstrap' communication in environments where an adversary is actively trying to disrupt signal synchronization.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to quantum optics and open quantum systems as it explores how entanglement-assisted protocols can provide resilience against adversarial noise (jamming) and demonstrates a super-activation-like phenomenon in bosonic channels.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the 'bootstrap' problem in communication: how a sender and receiver can establish shared randomness to stabilize an Arbitrarily Varying Channel (AVC) against a jammer when no pre-shared resources exist.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that using entangled Two-Mode Squeezed Vacuum (TMSV) states allows the sender and receiver to break channel symmetrizability and achieve positive communication capacity, even when a jammer can otherwise reduce the capacity to zero.

**Method.** The study uses Gaussian covariance matrices, Wigner functions, and information-theoretic bounds (like Pinsker's inequality) to analyze a correlation distribution procedure involving homodyne detection and sign binarization.

**Model / system.** A quantum-optical communication model using a beam splitter with transmissivity eta, involving coherent states for BPSK signaling and Two-Mode Squeezed Vacuum (TMSV) states as a quantum resource, subject to an energy-limited Gaussian jammer.

**Key observables.** Binarized correlation coefficient (rho_bin), quadrature measurements (x, p), and channel capacities (C, C_bar, C_tilde).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Squeezing magnitude (r), beam splitter transmissivity (eta), jamming energy (alpha^2), and BPSK amplitude (alpha).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The jammer is assumed to be energy-limited and restricted to using Gaussian states; the communication model is based on a standard optical setup.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 provides a visualization of the probability space of correlations, depicting the set of possible correlations within a convex polytope.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the jamming problem and the bootstrap challenge, describes the quantum-optical communication model and TMSV protocol, presents the mathematical proof for capacity enhancement and correlation persistence, and concludes with an appendix proving the monotonicity of binarized Gaussian correlations.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Shared randomness is the central ingredient for stabilizing symmetrizable communication systems against arbitrarily varying jammers. Given the presence of the jammer, however, the question arises how this precious resource could have been distributed. Several works discuss the use of external sources for this task. In this work, we show, based on the most standard optical communication model, how the sender and receiver can employ entangled two-mode squeezed states to counter the jamming attack of an energy-limited jammer during the distribution phase when both the sender and jammer are allowed to use binary phase shift keying and two-mode squeezed vacuum states.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.00341"></a>
### [Measuring the largest coefficients of a quantum state](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00341v1)

**Authors:** Nicolás Ciancaglini, Santiago Cifuentes, Guido Bellomo, Santiago Figueira, Ariel Bendersky  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.00341v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **3/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00341_figures/2605.00341_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Figure 1. Parent-child relationship in the tree. The set S is partitioned into the disjoint subsets S1 and S2. Likewise, the set S1 is partitioned into S11 and S12.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00341_figures/2605.00341_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Figure 2. We show some branches of the tree starting from the root, which corresponds to the set of all indices. We partition the set Γλ by considering the subsets of strings that start with I, X, Y or Z.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00341_figures/2605.00341_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> Figure 3. Circuit used to estimate Kµ using a mod- ification of the SWAP test.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00341_figures/2605.00341_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> Figure 4. Success probability of recovering (I⊗n + X⊗n)/2n for different values of n and different sam- ple sizes. Each data point is obtained by running 100 simulations and averaging.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00341_figures/2605.00341_fig5.jpg" alt="Figure 5">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 5.</b> Figure 5. Number of nodes visited when trying to recover (In + Xn)/2n for different values of n and different sample sizes. Each data point is obtained by running 100 simulations and averaging.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** The paper introduces a new algorithm to find the most significant components of a quantum state in the Pauli basis using a tree-based search. By avoiding full tomography, it offers an efficient way to reconstruct structured states like stabilizer states. The method's efficiency depends on the state's purity and sparsity.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a practical tool for the targeted characterization of large-scale quantum states, which is highly relevant for verifying many-body states and error-corrected systems in quantum computing.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Efficiently identifying and measuring the largest Pauli coefficients of an unknown n-qubit quantum state without the exponential cost of full state tomography.

**Main result.** The authors develop a hierarchical tree-based algorithm that can identify significant Pauli coefficients with a complexity bounded by the state's purity and the desired threshold, achieving near-optimal scaling for structured states like stabilizer states.

**Method.** A hierarchical algorithm using a prefix-based tree traversal where node weights are estimated via Bell sampling on two copies of the state or via modified SWAP tests.

**Model / system.** n-qubit quantum states, specifically focusing on sparse representations such as stabilizer states and Pauli-singleton states.

**Key observables.** Pauli coefficients (c_nu) and node weights (K_mu), which represent the sum of squared coefficients within a specific Pauli prefix subset.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Purity (Tr[rho^2]), coefficient threshold (epsilon), number of qubits (n), and the number of samples (M).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The algorithm assumes the state has a sparse or structured representation in the Pauli basis to avoid exponential node expansion; it also assumes access to two copies of the state for Bell sampling.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows tree partitioning; Figure 2 visualizes the prefix tree structure; Figure 3 shows the SWAP test circuit; Figures 4-7 present numerical results on success probabilities and node expansion scaling for Pauli-singleton and stabilizer states.

**Paper structure.** Introduction of the search problem, description of the hierarchical tree algorithm, derivation of node weight estimation methods (Bell sampling/SWAP tests), complexity analysis for finding coefficients above a threshold or the top t coefficients, and numerical validation using stabilizer and Pauli-singleton states.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We introduce a hierarchical algorithm for identifying the largest Pauli coefficients of an unknown $n$-qubit quantum state. The algorithm traverses a prefix-based tree whose nodes represent partial sums of squared Pauli coefficients, always expanding branches with the largest estimated weight and discarding the rest. Node weights are estimated using Bell sampling on two copies of the state, or alternatively via SWAP tests on subsystems. We analyze the sample complexity of each node estimation and derive bounds on the total number of nodes expanded as a function of the desired number of coefficients and the state's purity. For states admitting a sparse representation in the Pauli basis, the algorithm achieves a good reconstruction of the dominant components without requiring full state tomography. We validate the method with numerical simulations on Pauli-singleton states and random stabilizer states, showing that the algorithm's performance is competitive with other methods for structured states. Our work addresses an open problem in Pauli sampling and provides a practical tool for the targeted characterization of structured quantum states.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.00338"></a>
### [Staircase mechanical energy growth in optomechanical systems of median mechanical frequencies](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00338v1)

**Authors:** Yi Xiao, Yi Wu, Qi-Kai Zhan, Jin Lian Zhang, Bing He, Qing Lin  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.00338v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5**

**Summary.** This paper discovers a new 'staircase' pattern of mechanical energy growth in optomechanical systems operating at intermediate mechanical frequencies. It demonstrates how this pattern is sensitive to driving parameters and proposes its use for advanced sensing and phonon laser applications.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides new insights into the non-equilibrium dynamics of driven-dissipative systems and identifies a new dynamical regime that could be utilized for high-precision sensing and phonon laser generation.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates a previously unknown 'staircase' evolution pattern of mechanical energy in cavity optomechanical systems (COMS) when driven by a two-tone field in the intermediate frequency regime.

**Main result.** The authors identify a staircase-like energy growth pattern occurring when the mechanical frequency is comparable to the cavity damping rate, noting that this regime exhibits unique bifurcations and sensitive responses to frequency mismatches.

**Method.** The researchers used numerical simulations of coupled differential equations for the intracavity field and mechanical resonator, alongside analytical approximations using Bessel function expansions for sideband amplitudes.

**Model / system.** A cavity optomechanical system (COMS), such as a Fabry-Perot cavity with a movable mirror, driven by a two-tone laser field where the frequency difference matches the mechanical frequency.

**Key observables.** Mechanical energy (phonon number), mechanical amplitude, mechanical frequency, intracavity field intensity, and sideband amplitudes.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Mechanical frequency to cavity damping ratio (omega_m/kappa), mechanical quality factor (Q), drive tone powers, and frequency mismatch (delta).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis focuses on the intermediate regime where omega_m is neither much larger nor much smaller than kappa; the model assumes a specific two-tone driving condition.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the COMS setup and step-like displacement; Figure 2 compares energy evolution across resolved, unresolved, and intermediate regimes; Figure 3 examines the influence of the quality factor; Figure 6 illustrates the effects of positive and negative frequency mismatch.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the optomechanical system and the problem, compares different sideband regimes, details the discovery of the staircase pattern in the intermediate regime, analyzes the impact of system parameters like Q and power, explores frequency mismatch effects, and concludes with potential applications.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Owing to the radiation-force-induced nonlinearity, cavity optomechanical systems (COMS) exhibit dynamical phenomena such as back-action induced oscillation, chaos, mechanical amplitude locking, and anomalous stabilization, which occur under different driving conditions and different system parameters. We here identify a previously unknown dynamical pattern of staircase evolution for the energy of mechanical resonator, when a COMS with neither very large nor very small built-in mechanical frequency is driven by a two-tone field, which satisfies a condition that the frequency difference of the two tones matches the built-in mechanical frequency. The properties of this phenomenon are analyzed for the different system parameters due to fabrication such as mechanical frequencies and quality factors, as well as under the varied driving conditions such as unequal drive tone powers and mismatched drive tone difference from the mechanical frequency. Some special features, such as an emergent bifurcation due to the tone power difference, together with the totally different responses of the system to the drive tone mismatches of opposite signs, are discovered to exist only in this type of COMS with median mechanical frequencies. This work fills a gap in the study of the dynamics of COMS under two-tone drives. In the aspect of applications, the rapid increase of mechanical energy exhibited in the phenomenon promises phonon laser generation, and the sensitive dynamical response to the drive tone mismatches offers a potential approach to high-precision sensing.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.00331"></a>
### [Toward Heisenberg-Limited Interferometry with Dual Squeezers](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00331v1)

**Authors:** Yi Gu, Song-Ping Wang, Wei Zhong  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.00331v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

**Summary.** The paper proposes a new dual-squeezing interferometer design that overcomes the fundamental limitation where phase sensitivity diverges in standard setups. By adding a second squeezer at the output, the scheme achieves Heisenberg-limited sensitivity that remains robust even with imperfect detectors. This provides a feasible path toward high-precision quantum-limited measurements.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to quantum optics and quantum information as it proposes a practical, robust method to reach the fundamental Heisenberg limit in interferometry, overcoming the practical limitations of signal vanishing and detection loss.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Standard Mach-Zehnder interferometers (MZI) fail to achieve Heisenberg-limited phase sensitivity using direct photon-number-difference detection in the optimal equal-intensity regime due to a vanishing signal and divergence in sensitivity.

**Main result.** The introduction of a dual-squeezing approach (DS-MZI) eliminates the signal divergence and enables Heisenberg-limited phase sensitivity that is robust against detection noise and photon loss.

**Method.** The authors use an analytical framework involving SU(2) and SU(1,1) algebraic operators, error propagation formulas, and Wigner function analysis to evaluate phase sensitivity and robustness.

**Model / system.** A Dual-Squeezing Mach-Zehnder Interferometer (DS-MZI) consisting of two single-mode squeezers (one at the input and one at the output), beam splitters, and a phase shifter, fed with a coherent state and a squeezed-vacuum state.

**Key observables.** Photon-number-difference (N-), total photon-number (N+), and phase sensitivity (delta phi).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Squeezing parameters (r1, r2), detection efficiency (eta), input amplitude (alpha), and the equal-intensity regime (alpha^2 approx sinh^2 r).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The interferometer is assumed to be lossless for the derivation of the Quantum Fisher Information, and the optical elements are assumed to preserve Gaussian statistics.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the Wigner function distributions showing the elliptical distribution of the DS-MZI; Figure 3 compares the scaled phase sensitivity of the conventional MZI versus the DS-MZI under imperfect detection efficiency.

**Paper structure.** The paper identifies the divergence problem in standard MZI, introduces the DS-MZI architecture, provides an analytical derivation of phase sensitivity using operator algebra, evaluates performance under imperfect detection, and compares the results to the Heisenberg limit.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The canonical Mach-Zehnder interferometer fed with a coherent state and a squeezed-vacuum state of equal intensities is theoretically predicted to achieve Heisenberg scaling in phase sensitivity. However, this ultimate performance is unattainable using direct photon-number-difference detection due to a divergence arising precisely at the optimal equal-intensity regime. In this work, we introduce a dual-squeezing approach that overcomes this fundamental limitation. Our scheme employs an additional single-mode squeezer before detection, forming a paired configuration with the input squeezer used to generate the squeezed-vacuum state. We analytically demonstrate that the resulting dual-squeezing Mach-Zehnder interferometer enables Heisenberg-limited phase sensitivity with di rect photon-number-difference detection, while remaining robust against detection noise. Our work provides a feasible and robust route toward quantum-limited interferometric phase measurements

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.00739"></a>
### [A Resource-Efficient Variational Quantum Framework for the Traveling Salesman Problem](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00739v1)

**Authors:** Yuefeng Lin, Chao Zheng, Cong Guo  
**Type:** both · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.00739v1>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a resource-efficient quantum framework for solving the Traveling Salesman Problem by reducing qubit requirements from quadratic to near-linear scaling. By using a divide-and-conquer strategy and compact binary encoding, the authors demonstrate that even very small, noisy quantum hardware can successfully solve small-scale combinatorial optimization problems.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The quadratic qubit overhead of standard one-hot encoding makes implementing the Traveling Salesman Problem (TSP) impractical on NISQ hardware. The paper seeks to reduce qubit requirements and enable execution on extremely resource-constrained devices.

**Main result.** The proposed framework reduces qubit scaling from O(n^2) to O(n log n) and achieves success rates up to 100% in simulations. Hardware experiments on NMR quantum computers successfully identified optimal tours for a 5-city instance using a divide-and-conquer strategy.

**Method.** The authors use a compact binary-register encoding, a permutation-preserving ansatz with parameterized SWAP operations, and a divide-and-conquer strategy that factorizes the global state into smaller subsystems. Error mitigation techniques like Iterative Bayesian Unfolding (IBU) are also employed.

**Model / system.** The problem is modeled as a Traveling Salesman Problem (TSP) using a QUBO-derived Ising Hamiltonian. Experimental validation was performed on SpinQ Gemini Pro and SpinQ Triangulum II NMR quantum computers.

**Key observables.** Average success rate (finding the optimal tour), loss function value, and target-state probability.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of cities (n=4, 5, 6), circuit depth (L), penalty coefficients (lambda, mu), and number of optimization iterations.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The divide-and-conquer approach assumes a product-state approximation that neglects inter-subsystem entanglement. The benchmark is also limited to small-scale TSP instances.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the two-qubit variational circuit; Figure 2 plots success rate vs. circuit depth; Figure 3 compares the proposed ansatz to the one-hot method; Figures 4 and 5 show optimization trajectories on NMR hardware comparing different error mitigation methods.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the qubit overhead problem, proposes a compact encoding and permutation-preserving ansatz, details a divide-and-conquer execution strategy, presents numerical simulation results, and concludes with hardware validation on NMR quantum computers.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The Traveling Salesman Problem (TSP) is a prototypical combinatorial optimization problem, but its quantum implementation is limited by the O(n^2)-qubit overhead of standard one-hot encodings. Here, we propose a resource-efficient variational quantum framework based on compact binary-register encoding, a permutation-preserving problem-inspired ansatz, and a complementary divide-and-conquer execution strategy. The compact encoding reduces the data-qubit requirement to O(n log n), while the divide-and-conquer formulation lowers the number of qubits required in each local hardware execution to the size of the largest subsystem. Numerical simulations on TSP instances with 4, 5, and 6 cities achieve best average success rates of 100%, 100%, and 95.5%, respectively. A local two-qubit implementation of the divide-and-conquer approximation is further evaluated for a 5-city TSP instance on SpinQ Gemini Pro and SpinQ Triangulum II NMR quantum computers. Taken together, the results indicate how compact encoding and divide-and-conquer execution with classical post-processing can be used to study small combinatorial optimization instances on resource-constrained quantum hardware.

</details>

The dashed line denotes ρd/ρs = 0.5. The high temperature behavior of the drag for each q signals the critical nature of the transition away from superfluidity. For q ≲2.25 the transi- tion is driven by the disordering of single phases ϕ1, ϕ2, while for q ≳2.25 it is driven by disordering of one of the two linear combinations ϕ1 ± ϕ2. The line |ρd/ρs| = 0.5 is the marginal point where the single component superfluid stiffness is equal to one of the composite superfluid stiffnesses.</figcaption>

</div>

**Summary.** This paper uses Quantum Monte Carlo simulations to prove that fermions can form a 'quadrupling condensate' through correlated hopping. It overcomes the fermionic sign problem to show that these exotic four-fermion states are theoretically robust and likely observable in ultracold atomic gases.

**Why it may be interesting.** It demonstrates a way to numerically access exotic, non-BCS phases like charge-4e superconductivity and provides a roadmap for observing these states in ultracold atomic platforms.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating whether fermionic condensation can occur via higher-order 'quadruplets' (four-fermion order) rather than standard BCS pairing, and overcoming the numerical difficulty of the fermionic sign problem.

**Main result.** The study provides direct numerical evidence for a fermion-quadrupling condensate in a microscopic model, demonstrating that these exotic states are experimentally accessible in ultracold atomic gases.

**Method.** Large-scale Quantum Monte Carlo (QMC) simulations using the Worm algorithm, combined with a sign-free approximation and Renormalization Group (RG) analysis of vortex proliferation.

**Model / system.** A two-component fermionic lattice model (a generalization of the Penson-Kolb-Hubbard model) on hexagonal and square lattices, featuring correlated hopping and Andreev-Bashkin drag.

**Key observables.** Phase-stiffness matrix (including co-flow and counter-flow stiffness), four-fermion correlators, and vortex densities.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Strong-coupling regime (mu = -3.1, U = -6, t = 1, d = 1, q = 3); relative drag parameter |rho_d/rho_s|; and the transition driver parameter q.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The use of a sign-free approximation (treating fermions as hard-core bosons) is validated for certain observables, though it may not precisely locate the superfluid-quadrupling transition.

**Figures summary.** Figure 4 shows the relative difference in stiffnesses between the full fermionic model and the sign-free approximation, indicating negligible error for larger stiffnesses.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of higher-order fermionic order, presents a microscopic model with correlated hopping, describes the QMC and sign-mitigation methods, analyzes phase transitions via RG and vortex dynamics, and concludes with experimental implications for ultracold gases.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Fermionic condensation typically occurs via pairing. In recent decades, however, a fundamental question has emerged: whether alternative forms of order exist, such as condensates of fermion quadruplets. These states--including ``charge-4e" superconductors and ``charge-0" counterflow condensates--lie beyond the standard Bardeen-Cooper-Schrieffer framework, and require strong fluctuations and correlation effects that invalidate the BCS mean-field description. This makes the problem notoriously difficult to study numerically at a microscopic level, as it involves both strong interactions and the fermionic sign problem. Here, we present a microscopic fermionic model featuring correlated hopping that significantly mitigates the sign problem, enabling rigorous Monte-Carlo-based analysis. Using large-scale simulations, we demonstrate the existence of a fermion-quadrupling condensate with a transition temperature comparable to the hopping energy scale. These results provide direct numerical evidence for quartic fermionic order in a microscopic system and suggest that these exotic states are also experimentally accessible in ultracold atomic gases.

</details>

### [Provable and scalable quantum Gaussian processes for quantum learning](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00099v1)

**Authors:** Jonas Jäger, Paolo Braccia, Pablo Bermejo, Manuel G. Algaba, Diego García-Martín, M. Cerezo  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.00099v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5**

**Summary.** This paper introduces Quantum Gaussian Processes, a scalable Bayesian learning framework for quantum systems. By utilizing the structure of matchgate circuits, the authors demonstrate that one can perform efficient regression, classification, and optimization on large-scale quantum data even with very sparse training samples.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a rigorous bridge between Bayesian inference and quantum dynamics, offering a way to perform scalable learning on many-body systems using physics-informed priors.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Existing quantum machine learning approaches lack scalable, interpretable, and provable frameworks that leverage physics-informed inductive bias, especially when unknown unitaries act non-trivially on all qubits.

**Main result.** The authors introduce Quantum Gaussian Processes (QGP) and prove that matchgate (free-fermionic) circuits provide a provable and scalable family of QGPs. They also propose a robust 'lambda_Wigner-shift' method to handle kernel noise from finite-shot measurements.

**Method.** A non-parametric Bayesian framework using quantum kernels derived from the symmetries of the underlying quantum process. The authors use moment-based proofs, the Weingarten expansion, and marginal likelihood maximization for kernel optimization.

**Model / system.** The framework is applied to matchgate/free-fermionic evolutions (represented by the SPIN(2n) group) and tested on 1D many-body systems like the XXX and XXZ spin chains.

**Key observables.** Expectation values of Hermitian operators (e.g., Pauli strings, magnetization, and order-parameter proxies).

**Important parameters / regimes.** System size (n up to 100 qubits), shot budgets for observations (n_obs) and kernels (n_kappa), and Majorana sector indices (m).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The unknown unitary transformation is assumed to belong to a known group (e.g., SPIN(2n)), and observation noise is assumed to be Gaussian.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the QGP framework schematic; Figure 4 illustrates magnetization regression in XXZ chains; Figure 5 demonstrates Bayesian optimization trajectories and magnetometry tasks; Supplemental figures show MSE comparisons, Pareto fronts, and the effectiveness of the Wigner-shift method.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the QGP framework, proves the scalability and provability of matchgate-based QGPs, demonstrates applications in regression, classification, and optimization, and concludes with a discussion on noise robustness and kernel optimization.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Despite rapid recent advances in quantum machine learning, the field is in many ways stuck. Existing approaches can exhibit serious limitations, and we still lack learning frameworks that are simple, interpretable, scalable, and naturally suited to quantum data. To address this, here we introduce quantum Gaussian processes, a Bayesian framework for learning from quantum systems through priors over unknown quantum transformations. We show that, under suitable conditions, unitary quantum stochastic processes define Gaussian processes, thereby enabling regression, classification, and Bayesian optimization directly on quantum data. The key ingredient in this framework is sufficient knowledge of a quantum process's structure and symmetries to define an informative prior through its corresponding quantum kernel, effectively injecting a strong, physics-informed inductive bias into the learning model. We then prove that matchgate, or free-fermionic, evolutions give rise to provable and scalable quantum Gaussian processes, providing the first family in our framework where the unknown unitary acts non-trivially on all qubits. Finally, we demonstrate accurate long-range extrapolation, phase-diagram learning in many-body systems, and sample-efficient Bayesian optimization in a quantum sensing task. Our results identify quantum Gaussian processes as a promising route toward simpler and more structured forms of quantum learning.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.00784"></a>
### [The structure of gauge invariant Gaussian quantum operations on finite Fermion systems](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00784v1)

**Authors:** Eric A. Carlen  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.00784v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **2/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00784_figures/2605.00784_page2.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Low-resolution page preview, page 2</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00784_figures/2605.00784_page3.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Low-resolution page preview, page 3</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00784_figures/2605.00784_page4.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> Low-resolution page preview, page 4</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00784_figures/2605.00784_page5.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> Low-resolution page preview, page 5</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00784_figures/2605.00784_page6.jpg" alt="Figure 5">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 5.</b> Low-resolution page preview, page 6</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper provides a complete structural characterization of quantum operations and semigroups that preserve gauge-invariant Gaussian states in finite Fermion systems. It proves that one-to-one operations are determined by specific symbol maps and shows that such operations can be naturally extended to the full CAR algebra. The results establish a precise correspondence between GIG semigroups and pairs of operators acting on the single-particle space.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to open quantum systems and many-body dynamics as it provides a rigorous classification of noise processes (quantum operations) that preserve the Gaussian structure of Fermionic systems, which is a cornerstone of many-body physics and quantum information processing.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to characterize the structure of quantum operations (CPTP maps) and semigroups on finite-dimensional Fermion systems that preserve the set of gauge-invariant Gaussian (GIG) states.

**Main result.** The author provides a structure theorem for one-to-one quantum operations and semigroups, showing they are parameterized by pairs (G, A) where G is a contraction semigroup generator and 0 <= A <= -G - G*, and demonstrates how these operations extend to the full CAR algebra.

**Method.** The study utilizes the algebraic framework of the Canonical Anti-commutation Relations (CAR), second quantization, symbol maps for Gaussian states, and tools from operator theory such as the Evans-Hugenholtz-Kadison construction and modular theory.

**Model / system.** The system consists of finite-dimensional Fermion systems defined by a single-particle Hilbert space H1 and a corresponding Fock space K, governed by the CAR algebra and a gauge automorphism group.

**Key observables.** The number operator (N), the symbol (Q) representing the covariance/two-point function, and the particle-hole automorphism.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The dimension of the single-particle space, the contraction semigroup generator G, and the operator A satisfying the stability condition.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary structure theorem assumes the quantum operations are one-to-one; the study is restricted to finite-dimensional Fermion systems.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by defining the algebraic framework of the CAR field and gauge structure, proceeds to classify GIG states via symbols, establishes structure theorems for one-to-one quantum operations and semigroups, and concludes with results on the extension of these maps to the full CAR algebra and the properties of conditional expectations.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Let ${\mathcal H}_1$ be a finite dimensional complex Hilbert space. Let $ψ\mapsto Z(ψ)$ be a canonical anti-commutation relations (CAR) field over ${\mathcal H}_1$ acting irreducibly on a Hilbert space ${\mathord{\mathscr K}}$. The $*$-algebra ${\mathscr A}_{{\mathcal H}_1}$ generated by the $Z(ψ)$, $ψ\in {\mathcal H}_1$, is simply all operators on ${\mathscr K}$.   However, the CAR field endows ${\mathscr A}_{{\mathcal H}_1}$ with additional structure, and we are concerned with quantum operations whose acting in harmony with this structure. In particular, there is a gauge automorphism group   generated by ``second quantizing'' $ψ\mapsto e^{it}ψ$. The fixed point algebra of the gauge group, ${\mathscr G}_{{\mathcal H}_1}$, is a sub-algebra of ${\mathscr A}_{{\mathcal H}_1}$ studied by Araki and Wyss. It contains the density matrices of an important class of states, the gauge invariant Gaussian states, ${\mathfrak S}_{GIG}$.   Our focus is on semigroups $\{e^{t{\mathscr L}}\}_{t\geq 0}$ of quantum operations on ${\mathscr A}_{{\mathcal H}_1}$ that map ${\mathfrak S}_{GIG}$ into itself. Each $e^{t{\mathscr L}}$ is one-to-one, and our first main result is a structure theorem forsuch quantum operations on ${\mathscr G}_{{\mathcal H}_1}$ that map ${\mathfrak S}_{GIG}$ into itself.   We apply this to study semigroups of quantum operations on ${\mathscr G}_{{\mathcal H}_1}$ that map ${\mathfrak S}_{GIG}$ into itself. Our second main result is a structure theorem showing that they are parameterized by pairs $(G,A)$ where $G$ is a contraction semigroup generator on ${\mathcal H}_1$, and $0 \leq A \leq -G -G^*$. We then show that each of these semigroups has a natural extension to the full CAR algebra ${\mathscr A}_{{\mathcal H}_1}$. Further results are obtained under further assumptions on the pair $(G,A)$.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.00127"></a>
### [Comment on "Controlling the dynamical evolution of quantum coherence and quantum correlations in $e^{+} e^{-} \rightarrow Λ\barΛ$ processes at BESIII''](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00127v1)

**Authors:** Saeed Haddadi  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.00127v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **2/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00127_figures/2605.00127_page2.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Low-resolution page preview, page 2</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper critiques a recent study on quantum correlations in hyperon-antihyperon production, arguing that the use of open quantum system theory is physically unjustified. The author shows that because the particles do not share an environment and cannot be subjected to controlled measurements, the reported dynamical evolution and steering are conceptually flawed. The work emphasizes the need to distinguish between mathematical quantifiers and physically realizable quantum information protocols.

**Why it may be interesting.** This paper provides a critical cautionary tale for researchers applying open quantum system formalisms (like non-Markovianity and steering) to high-energy physics systems where the necessary environmental coupling and measurement control are absent.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The author critiques the physical validity of applying open quantum system frameworks, such as correlated quantum channels and non-Markovian dynamics, to the hyperon-antihyperon (Lambda-antiLambda) system.

**Main result.** The author demonstrates that the analyzed work is physically inconsistent because the produced particles are free, unstable, and do not interact with a shared environment, making the reported quantum correlations and steering operationally meaningless.

**Method.** The author uses a theoretical critique of the underlying physical assumptions, specifically addressing the lack of a shared bath, the absence of information backflow, and the impossibility of real-time measurement-induced state updates in relativistic particle decays.

**Model / system.** The system consists of a Lambda-antiLambda (Lambda-antiLambda) pair produced via the electron-positron annihilation process e+e- -> Lambda-antiLambda, treated as a bipartite spin system.

**Key observables.** spin correlations, quantum coherence, entanglement, and quantum steering

**Assumptions / limitations.** The author assumes that the particles are free, unstable, relativistic particles that do not interact with a common environment after production.

**Paper structure.** The paper is structured as a series of four remarks and a main point, each addressing a specific physical inconsistency regarding correlated channels, Markovianity, quantum steering, and the hierarchy of correlations.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We critically examine recent claims [Phys. Rev. D 113, 016024 (2026)] regarding quantum coherence, steering, and non-Markovian dynamics in the hyperon-antihyperon system produced in the process $e^{+} e^{-} \rightarrow Λ\barΛ$. We argue that the theoretical framework employed in the analyzed work suffers from fundamental physical inconsistencies. In particular, the treatment of the $Λ\barΛ$ pair as a bipartite system evolving under correlated quantum channels is not physically justified, since the produced hyperons are free, unstable particles that do not interact with a common environment after production. Consequently, the application of open quantum system techniques, including Markovian and non-Markovian quantum channels, lacks a clear physical basis. Moreover, we show that the computation and interpretation of quantum steering for this system is operationally and conceptually meaningless, as no well-defined measurement-induced state update or controllable local measurement scenario exists for unstable relativistic particles. These issues call into question the physical relevance of the reported quantum correlations, their hierarchy, and their dynamical behavior. Our analysis highlights the necessity of carefully distinguishing between formal mathematical quantifiers of quantumness and physically realizable quantum information protocols in high-energy particle systems.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper provides a new graph-theoretic proof for the mutual linearity of transient probabilities and hitting time distributions in Markov networks. By using the all-minorable matrix-tree theorem, the authors derive explicit combinatorial formulas for how these distributions respond to changes in transition rates. The results bridge the gap between stationary and non-stationary dynamics and show that short-time responses are determined by graph geometry.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides fundamental mathematical constraints on the dynamics of stochastic networks, which is highly relevant for understanding the relaxation and response of open quantum systems and biochemical networks.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper seeks to establish a graph-theoretic derivation for the 'mutual linearity' property in Markov networks, extending it from the stationary regime to the non-stationary (transient) regime in Laplace space.

**Main result.** The authors prove that mutual linearity applies to both transient occupation probabilities and hitting time distributions, providing explicit combinatorial expressions for non-stationary response ratios and susceptibilities.

**Method.** The study employs the all-minors matrix-tree theorem, Laplace transforms, and combinatorial tools like spanning forests and trees on augmented graphs to derive the results.

**Model / system.** The system consists of irreducible, time-homogeneous Markov networks defined by a continuous-time stochastic process on a directed, edge-weighted graph where dynamics are governed by the Kolmogorov forward equation.

**Key observables.** Occupation probabilities, network currents, response ratios, susceptibility, and hitting time distributions.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Transition rates (k_ij), Laplace variable (s), stationary regime (long-time limit), and short-time asymptotics (governed by minimal path distances).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The Markov networks are assumed to be irreducible and time-homogeneous.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 visualizes graph theoretic concepts like directed graphs and spanning forests; Figure 5 illustrates an auxiliary map for edge exchange; Figure 6 shows short-time scaling of response ratios in an example network.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of mutual linearity, develops a graph-theoretic framework using the all-minors matrix-tree theorem, extends the derivation to the non-stationary regime in Laplace space, proves the extension to hitting time densities, and analyzes short-time and long-time limits.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

For irreducible, time-homogeneous Markov networks, mutual linearity has recently been established for both occupation probabilities and network currents in the stationary regime as well as in the non-stationary regime in Laplace space. The derivation of this property for the stationary distribution utilized the Markov-chain tree theorem, which also allows for an explicit combinatorial expression of the response ratios under variation of a single transition rate. The extension of this result was proven at the trajectory level by employing the Doob-Meyer decomposition. By employing the all-minors matrix-tree theorem, we show that this property also follows from a graph theoretic formulation and derive explicit combinatorial expressions for the non-stationary response ratios. The stationary result follows as the long-time limit and we also show that the small-time asymptotics are entirely determined by minimal path distances in the underlying graph. Finally we use the graph theoretic approach to prove that mutual linearity also extends to hitting time densities.

</details>

**Summary.** This review provides a structured framework to evaluate the evidence for quantum effects in biological processes and the potential for biological structures to advance quantum technology. It categorizes research into three directions: quantum in biology, quantum for biology, and biology for quantum. The paper highlights the need for decisive experiments to distinguish true quantum functionality from classical or semiclassical alternatives.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a high-level synthesis of open quantum system dynamics, specifically how environmental noise, dissipation, and vibronic coupling interact with biological structures, which is highly relevant to researchers in open quantum systems and quantum biology.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper seeks to map the intersection of quantum science and biology across three dimensions: quantum in biology, quantum for biology, and biology for quantum, specifically evaluating whether quantum resources are functionally necessary for biological processes.

**Main result.** The authors establish a structured evidence map and a qualitative 'evidence ladder' that categorizes biological phenomena from established quantum-chemical contributions (e.g., enzyme tunneling, radical-pair magnetoreception) to speculative proposals (e.g., microtubule quantum computing).

**Method.** The review employs a structured narrative evidence map using a five-criteria analytical framework: central claim, invoked quantum resource, strength of evidence, competing classical alternatives, and required decisive benchmarks.

**Model / system.** The paper covers a diverse range of biological and bio-inspired systems, including enzymatic hydrogen transfer, radical-pair spin chemistry in cryptochromes, photosynthetic excitonic dynamics, DNA replication, and biohybrid quantum platforms like DNA origami.

**Key observables.** Kinetic isotope effects (KIE), temperature dependence, hyperfine/Zeeman interactions, vibrational wavepackets, spin-dependent dynamics, and quantum yield.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Dephasing rates, vibronic coupling, tunneling probabilities, redox landscapes, and physiological conditions (temperature, pH, and noise).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The review assumes that distinguishing true quantum coherence from vibrational or ensemble effects is a primary challenge and that the utility of quantum tools must be measured against strong classical baselines.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 presents a conceptual framework for evaluating the three review directions; Figure 2 provides an overview of key topic areas in quantum biology; Tables III and IV summarize the evidence, challenges, and benchmarks for quantum-for-biology and biology-for-quantum, respectively.

**Paper structure.** The paper is organized into a three-way mapping (Quantum in/for/from Biology), using an evidence ladder to categorize topics, followed by detailed analysis of specific biological mechanisms, technological utilities, and bio-inspired engineering, concluding with a navigation table of evidence and benchmarks.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum science and biology now intersect in three complementary directions: quantum in biology, quantum for biology, and biology for quantum. This review provides a structured narrative evidence map of that interface rather than an exhaustive catalogue or formal systematic review. For each topic, we ask what the mechanistic or technological claim is, which quantum resource is invoked, what the strongest experiments and models establish, which classical alternatives or engineering confounds remain competitive, and what decisive tests or benchmarks would most strongly change confidence. The most mature quantum-in-biology cases remain mechanistically constrained tunneling in some enzymatic hydrogen-transfer reactions and radical-pair spin chemistry as a viable framework for magnetoreception, whereas several higher-visibility topics remain suggestive but unresolved under physiological conditions. In quantum for biology, the central issue is whether quantum-enabled tools improve biological inference relative to strong classical baselines under realistic calibration, dose, throughput, and uncertainty constraints. In biology for quantum, the strongest claims arise when biomolecular structure or self-assembly measurably improves fabrication, integration, or robustness in quantum devices. Summary tables in the Appendix provide a compact cross-map view of the current evidence, major confounds, and the experiments or benchmarks most likely to discriminate between competing explanations.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper proposes using confinement modulation to implement continuous dynamical decoupling for spin qubits during transport. By periodically changing the qubit's potential, the researchers show they can suppress low-frequency noise in germanium hole-spin systems. This provides a practical method for creating noise-resilient quantum links in large-scale quantum computers.

**Why it may be interesting.** This paper is highly relevant to open quantum systems researchers as it explores the control of decoherence in a driven-dissipative system using continuous dynamical decoupling and examines the interplay between control protocols and the spatial/temporal scales of environmental noise.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The goal is to suppress spin qubit decoherence caused by low-frequency magnetic and electric noise during the long-range shuttling of qubits in scalable quantum architectures.

**Main result.** The study demonstrates that continuous dynamical decoupling (CDD) via confinement modulation (temporal or spatial breathing) can significantly enhance coherence by rotating the spin to mitigate noise, with the effectiveness depending on the noise correlation length.

**Method.** The authors use the filter function formalism, Schrieffer-Wolff transformations, and the Magnus expansion to analyze the susceptibility of the driven two-level system to various noise sources.

**Model / system.** The system consists of germanium (Ge) hole-spin qubits in a conveyor-mode shuttling architecture, modeled as a driven two-level system with strong spin-orbit interaction and a position-dependent g-tensor.

**Key observables.** Average gate infidelity (I), transition probabilities, filter functions (F), and the ratio of infidelity without driving to infidelity with driving (I*/I).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Rabi frequency (Omega), Larmor frequency (omega_q), driving frequency (omega_d), driving amplitude (gamma), shuttling velocity (v), and noise correlation length (l_c).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the orbital adiabatic limit, the weak spin-orbit interaction limit, and that the driving frequency is much smaller than the orbital splitting; it also assumes 1/f noise power spectral density.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 sketches the shuttling architecture and breathing protocols; Figure 2 shows heatmaps of infidelity ratios and examples of filter functions; Figure 6 illustrates the infidelity ratio as a function of shuttling velocity and driving amplitude.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the shuttling problem, defines the physical model of Ge hole-spin qubits, develops the effective Hamiltonian via unitary transformations, analyzes noise suppression using filter functions for both global and local noise, and concludes with numerical simulations of the protocols.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Reliable long-range qubit shuttling is a powerful tool for scalable quantum computing architectures. We investigate strategies to improve the coherence of moving spin qubits by performing continuous dynamical decoupling by modulating their confinement potential. Specifically, we introduce temporal and spatial breathing shuttling protocols that leverage spin-orbit interactions in hole-spin systems to electrically drive the qubit while moving. This enables efficient dressed-state shuttling, where the spin is continuously rotated during transport, suppressing the effect of low-frequency noise. Using the filter function formalism, we identify driving regimes that efficiently mitigate both global and local magnetic and electric noise sources. We find that confinement-modulated shuttling can significantly enhance coherence during transport, while revealing distinct limitations depending on the correlation length of the noise. Applying our framework to germanium hole-spin qubits, we show that these protocols provide a practical route toward noise-resilient long-range coherent quantum links.

</details>

### [Bell Correlations and Selection Bias](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00406v1)

**Authors:** Huw Price  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.00406v1>

<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Figure 1: Survivorship bias.3 As we will explain, there are two ways to describe this conclusion. One is to say that the proposal avoids nonlocality, preserving locality without sacrificing realism. The other is to say that Bell nonlocality itself turns out to be a selection artefact. This is a terminological issue, depending on what we choose to mean by ‘nonlocality’ when the new option is in view. We will return to this question below (§4.1), referring to Bell’s own discussion of the term. Either way, the upshot is that Bell correlations are much less puzzling than they have seemed, in tension neither with relativity nor realism. We stress that ‘less puzzling’ does not imply ‘not puzzling...</figcaption>

<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> Figure 3: A common cause The second reason does apply in the familiar, causal world. A well-known property of common causes (see Figure 3) is that their effect on correlations is the opposite of a collider. The correlations they produce disappear if we condition on the common cause – if we select cases in which the common cause variable takes one particular value. The explanation is simple. If K co-varies with L because they both co-vary with a common cause J, then if we remove the variation of J, holding it fixed, we remove the co-variation between K and L. Here’s an example. Suppose that K and L are rare diseases of mice. Both diseases are genetic disorders, caused by different...</figcaption>

The real Bell experiment we had in mind can be performed with any of the four so-called Bell states as the initial preparation. Let’s write these four states as C0, C1, C2, C3, where C0 is the singlet state. (See Figure 5.) For each of these states, there is a probability distribution P(A,B|a,b,Ci) giving the predicted joint probability of outcomes (A,B) given settings (a,b) and initial state Ci. **Summary.** This paper proposes that the apparent nonlocality in quantum mechanics is actually a statistical artifact caused by selection bias. By treating Bell correlations as 'correlator bias,' the author suggests that local realism and special relativity can be preserved without needing to invoke spooky action at a distance.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the tension between Bell correlations and the principles of local realism and special relativity, questioning if these correlations necessitate nonlocality.

**Main result.** The author proposes that Bell correlations are not evidence of fundamental nonlocality but are instead 'selection artefacts' or 'correlator bias' arising from conditioning on a specific variable.

**Method.** The author uses a statistical framework of selection bias (specifically collider bias) and directed acyclic graphs (DAGs) to re-interpret Bell's theorem.

**Model / system.** The paper analyzes standard Bell-type experiments, including V-shaped (entangled pair preparation) and W-shaped (entanglement swapping) geometries, using classical and quantum toy models of selection bias.

**Key observables.** Bell correlations (violations of Bell inequalities) and the factorizability of joint probabilities.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The distinction between a 'super-ensemble' (full population) and a 'sub-ensemble' (selected subset) defined by a Correlator.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The proposal does not provide a physical mechanism for the underlying correlations and relies on the assumption that a randomizer can model the unconstrained super-ensemble.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows WWII bomber survivorship bias; Figures 2 and 3 show DAGs for colliders and common causes; Figure 4 shows a Bell experiment setup; Figure 5 shows Bell states; Figure 6 illustrates entanglement swapping (W-shaped) geometry.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces selection bias, applies it to Bell's theorem via 'correlator bias,' develops classical and quantum toy models, extends the argument to entanglement swapping (W-shaped experiments), and discusses implications for time-symmetric causality.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Selection artefacts are common in science. A method of selecting samples from a larger population may produce bias, in either direction. It may induce correlations between variables independent in the full population, or mask correlations between variables dependent in the full population. Here we propose a surprising application of these familiar ideas. We argue that they are relevant to puzzling correlations uncovered in quantum theory by John Stewart Bell (Bell 1964). In the light of Bell's work and subsequent experiments it is widely believed that the quantum world is 'nonlocal', in apparent tension with relativity. Many hold that the only alternative is to abandon 'realism', the view that there is an objective world independent of measurement. We propose instead that Bell correlations are selection artefacts, in tension neither with relativity nor realism.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Escaping Mode Collapse in LLM Generation via Geometric Regulation](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00435v1)

(a) Without RMR, geometric collapse begins around t ≈1000: the leading eigenvalue rises toward 1 while the second eigenvalue quickly drops toward 0, indicating a widening spectral gap and dominance by a single persistent mode. (b) With RMR, the leading eigenvalue in each layer is regulated below the threshold (λmin = 0.8), maintaining a stable spectrum and preventing collapse.</figcaption>

</figure>

**Summary.** This paper reinterprets LLM mode collapse as a geometric collapse of internal representation trajectories into low-dimensional attractors. It introduces RMR, a method to suppress persistent directions in the Transformer cache, effectively preventing repetitive generation. The approach maintains high text quality while enabling stable generation at much lower entropy rates.

**Why it may be interesting.** The paper uses tools from dynamical systems and statistical mechanics, such as Iterated Function Systems, phase transitions, and fractal dimensions, to analyze the stability and ergodicity of a high-dimensional stochastic process.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Addressing mode collapse in Large Language Models (LLMs), where autoregressive generation enters repetitive, low-entropy loops or loses semantic diversity.

**Main result.** The proposed Reinforced Mode Regulation (RMR) significantly increases non-collapse rates in LLMs (e.g., from 8% to 56% at temperature 0.7) without degrading text quality, by preventing the geometric collapse of internal trajectories.

**Method.** A lightweight, online intervention called Reinforced Mode Regulation (RMR) that uses low-rank damping to attenuate dominant, self-reinforcing directions in the Transformer value cache identified via a generalized eigenvalue problem.

**Model / system.** The system is modeled as an autoregressive Transformer-based LLM (e.g., Qwen, Llama) where the generation process is viewed as a stochastic trajectory in a high-dimensional state space (the KV cache), specifically using an Iterated Function System (IFS) framework to represent the dynamics.

**Key observables.** Correlation dimension (fractal dimension of the trajectory), next-token entropy, Distinct-n, and generalized eigenvalues of the value-cache subspace.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Temperature (T), damping strength (eta), eigenvalue threshold (lambda_min = 0.8), and entropy-locked/temperature-locked decoding regimes.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The method assumes access to the internal Transformer KV cache and relies on the approximation that the high-dimensional vocabulary can be binned for efficient computation.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 visualizes trajectory transitions in an IFS; Figure 2 shows explicit looping examples; Figure 4 shows the widening spectral gap signaling collapse; Figure 6 illustrates non-looping degeneration (template/conceptual looping); Figure 8 and 9 show the stability of correlation dimension under RMR.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of mode collapse, proposes a dynamical-systems reinterpretation as geometric collapse, presents the RMR method and its efficient implementation, validates the method using correlation dimension as a diagnostic, and demonstrates performance gains across various LLMs and temperatures.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Mode collapse is a persistent challenge in generative modeling and appears in autoregressive text generation as behaviors ranging from explicit looping to gradual loss of diversity and premature trajectory convergence. We take a dynamical-systems view and reinterpret mode collapse as reduced state-space accessibility caused by *geometric collapse*: during generation, the model's internal trajectory becomes confined to a low-dimensional region of its representation space. This implies mode collapse is not purely a token-level phenomenon and cannot be reliably solved by symbolic constraints or probability-only decoding heuristics. Guided by this perspective, we propose *Reinforced Mode Regulation* (RMR), a lightweight, online state-space intervention that regulates dominant self-reinforcing directions in the Transformer value cache (implemented as low-rank damping). Across multiple large language models, RMR substantially reduces mode collapse and enables stable, high-quality generation at extremely low entropy rates (down to 0.8 nats/step), whereas standard decoding typically collapses near 2.0 nats/step.

</details>

### [From Tensor Networks to Tractable Circuits, and back](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00106v1)

**Summary.** This paper bridges the gap between tensor networks and knowledge compilation by proving they are mathematically equivalent representations of the same class of functions. This allows physicists to use advanced algorithmic tools from formal verification, such as variable reordering and canonical forms, to optimize tensor network computations.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Establishing formal mathematical equivalences between the Tensor Network community (used in physics/ML) and the Tractable Circuits community (used in formal verification) to enable the transfer of algorithms and structural properties.

**Main result.** The paper proves that Matrix Product States (Tensor Trains) are equivalent to non-deterministic edge-valued decision diagrams, and Tree Tensor Networks correspond to structured-decomposable circuits, allowing for bidirectional transfer of complexity results and heuristics.

**Method.** The authors use the language of semi-rings to unify weights and develop constructive, polynomial-time transformation procedures between the two frameworks.

**Model / system.** The paper focuses on mathematical data structures for representing pseudo-Boolean functions, specifically Matrix Product States (MPS), Tree Tensor Networks (TTN), and Edge-Valued Decision Diagrams (EVDD).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The transformation from EVDD to TT assumes EVDDs are complete (all variables are read on every path), though this can be enforced efficiently.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the structural mapping between a structured-decomposable circuit and a tree tensor network; Figure 2 demonstrates the functional equivalence between an nd-EVDD and a Tensor Train.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of isolated frameworks, defines the mathematical models for both sides using semi-rings, proves specific structural equivalences (TT to EVDD and TTN to circuits), provides complexity analysis for transformations, and discusses the implications for cross-domain utility like variable ordering and lower bounds.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Tensor networks and circuits are widely used data structures to represent pseudo-Boolean functions. These two formalisms have been studied primarily in separate communities, and this paper aims to establish equivalences between them. We show that some classes of tensor networks that are appealing in practice correspond to classes of circuits with specific properties that have been studied in knowledge compilation as \emph{tractable circuits}. In particular, we prove that matrix product states (tensor trains) coincide with nondeterministic edge-valued decision diagrams and that tree tensor networks exactly correspond to structured-decomposable circuits. These correspondences enable direct transfer of structural and algorithmic results; for example, canonicity and tractability guarantees known for circuits yield analogous guarantees for the associated tensor networks, and vice versa.

</details>
