<a id="paper-2605.00129"></a>
### [Comment on "Quantum teleportation, entanglement, LQU and LQFI in $e^{+} e^{-} \rightarrow \mathrm{Y} \overline{\mathrm{Y}}$ processes at BESIII through noisy channels''](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00129v1)

**Authors:** Saeed Haddadi  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.00129v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00129_figures/2605.00129_page2.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Low-resolution page preview, page 2</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper provides a critique of applying quantum information theory to high-energy physics, arguing that metrics like teleportation fidelity and decoherence models lack physical meaning when applied to hyperon pairs. The author contends that these quantities should be viewed as descriptions of static production correlations rather than dynamical quantum resources or environmental effects.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper critiques the physical validity of applying standard quantum information metrics and decoherence models to high-energy particle production processes.

**Main result.** The author argues that using noise channels and teleportation fidelity to describe hyperon-antihyperon pairs is physically unjustified because there is no identifiable environment-system interaction and the particles cannot be used for operational quantum protocols.

**Method.** A critical theoretical analysis of the physical correspondence between standard CPTP noise models (like amplitude damping) and the dynamics of relativistic, unstable particles produced in scattering events.

**Model / system.** Hyperon-antihyperon (Y anti-Y) pairs produced via e+ e- annihilation at the BESIII experiment, characterized by a reconstructed spin density matrix.

**Key observables.** Logarithmic negativity, Local Quantum Uncertainty (LQU), Local Quantum Fisher Information (LQFI), and teleportation fidelity.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Abstract noise parameters used in the criticized study to model decoherence.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The author assumes that the validity of quantum information measures depends on the existence of a physical environment and the ability to perform operational control, which is absent in this high-energy system.

**Paper structure.** The paper presents a critique of a recent study by first identifying the mismatch between standard decoherence models and the physics of free-propagating unstable particles, then arguing against the operational interpretation of teleportation fidelity, and finally proposing a re-interpretation of information metrics as static production correlations.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We provide a critical assessment of a recent study applying quantum information concepts, including noisy channels and teleportation fidelity, to hyperon-antihyperon pairs produced in $e^{+}e^{-} \to Y\bar Y$ reactions at BESIII. While the spin density matrix reconstructed from experimental data provides a physically meaningful description of production correlations, we argue that its subsequent interpretation in terms of standard decoherence models-such as amplitude damping, phase damping, and phase flip-lacks a clear physical correspondence for these systems. The produced particles emerge from a single scattering event and propagate as free, unstable relativistic states, without a well-defined system-environment interaction acting on their spin degrees of freedom. As a result, the variation of quantum correlations with an abstract noise parameter does not describe a genuine physical evolution. We further contend that the reported teleportation fidelity should not be interpreted as evidence for operational quantum communication, since hyperon states cannot be prepared, controlled, or measured in a way that would enable a realizable teleportation protocol. More generally, quantities such as logarithmic negativity, local quantum uncertainty, and local quantum Fisher information primarily characterize static production correlations rather than directly usable quantum resources. Our analysis highlights the importance of distinguishing between formal quantum-information measures and their physical interpretation in high-energy particle systems.

</details>

### [Dynamical magnetotropic susceptibility as a new probe of Kitaev materials and beyond](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00568v1)

**Summary.** This paper introduces the dynamical magnetotropic susceptibility as a novel experimental probe for ultra-low-frequency fluctuations in quantum materials. It demonstrates how this probe can uniquely identify Kitaev physics and study charge fluctuations in metals. The work combines analytical derivation with advanced machine-learning-enhanced Monte Carlo simulations.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing a theoretical and numerical framework to use dynamical magnetotropic susceptibility as a new probe for the low-energy, uniform (q=0) spin and charge fluctuations in Kitaev materials and other correlated systems.

**Main result.** The study demonstrates that the magnetotropic susceptibility can identify Kitaev exchange dominance via a specific scaling law (k(0)/T = f(B/T)) and can distinguish between different metallic states and spin-orbit coupling effects.

**Method.** The authors use linear response theory to derive the susceptibility and employ auxiliary-field quantum Monte Carlo (AFQMC) with machine-learning-based optimization to mitigate the sign problem.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on Kitaev materials like alpha-RuCl3, insulating spin systems, and 2D metallic systems (e.g., PdCoO2), using a generic correlated-electron Hamiltonian that includes spin and charge degrees of freedom.

**Key observables.** Dynamical magnetotropic susceptibility k(omega), specifically its real part (frequency shift) and imaginary part (damping/energy absorption).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Magnetic field (B), temperature (T), magnetic anisotropy, and exchange couplings (Kitaev, Heisenberg, and Gamma terms).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The numerical approach is limited by the fermion sign problem at very low temperatures; the study assumes the weak-field limit for certain metallic models.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the experimental cantilever setup; Figure 3 compares static susceptibility scaling for different parameter sets; Figure 4 shows the dynamical spectrum k''(omega); Figure 6 illustrates cantilever voltage suppression and a phase diagram in UTe2; Figure 7 and 8 show correlation ratios and spin susceptibility for the XXZ model.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the magnetotropic susceptibility, derives its properties via linear response theory, applies it to insulating spin systems and metallic systems, performs numerical simulations on Kitaev models using ML-optimized AFQMC, and discusses broader applications to quantum criticality.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The magnetotropic susceptibility $k(ω)$ probes ultra-low-frequency uniform fluctuations. For a crystal mounted on an oscillating cantilever in a magnetic field, it is defined as the ratio of torque to angular-displacement amplitude. Its real and imaginary parts determine the oscillation-frequency shift and crystal-induced damping. It is a low-energy probe of uniform $q=0$ spin and charge degrees of freedom. We demonstrate this by deriving $k(ω)$ within linear response theory for a generic correlated-electron Hamiltonian with charge and spin degrees of freedom. Although it covers metallic and insulating magnets, correlated paramagnets, and exotic quantum critical points, we focus on limiting cases. For insulating spin systems $k(0)$ is sensitive to magnetic anisotropy whereas its finite-frequency imaginary part probes uniform dynamical spin susceptibility even in spin-symmetric models. For metallic systems we identify when eddy currents cause low-frequency damping. Our numerical results focus on Kitaev-material magnetotropic response. Using auxiliary-field quantum Monte Carlo with machine-learning-based sign-problem optimization we compute $k(ω)$ for several models proposed for $α$-RuCl$_3$. The observed low-temperature scaling of $k(0)/T$ with $B/T$ results from dominant Kitaev couplings: parameter sets without dominant Kitaev coupling do not exhibit this scaling. It remains robust upon inclusion of optical phonons. Beyond the static response, $k''(ω)$ for the $α$-RuCl$_3$ parameter set reproducing the experimental $k(0)$ data shows local-moment features at high and low $T$, with a single peak at the Larmor frequency. Beyond Kitaev systems we highlight broader applications. Probing ultra-low-energy uniform charge fluctuations is pertinent to Kondo destruction quantum criticality, of broad interest in strange metallicity and unconventional superconductivity.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper presents a new exact analytical solution for vortices in a 2D Bose gas including Lee-Huang-Yang corrections. It demonstrates that these vortex solutions are stable and describes how their structure and current density scale with topological charge. This result serves as a reliable theoretical benchmark for studying low-dimensional quantum fluids.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a rare exact analytical benchmark for 2D quantum fluids with beyond-mean-field effects, which is crucial for interpreting experimental observations of BKT transitions and stabilized quantum droplets.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The lack of exact analytical solutions for vortex structures in two-dimensional quantum gases when including Lee-Huang-Yang (LHY) beyond-mean-field corrections.

**Main result.** The derivation of an exact analytical vortex solution that provides a clear framework for understanding vortex structures, stability, and energy in 2D quantum fluids.

**Method.** The authors use an ansatz for a low-energy angular momentum eigenstate and apply the Lambert W function to solve the resulting transcendental equations, alongside the Newton-Mercator series expansion.

**Model / system.** A two-dimensional Bose liquid or ultra-cold atomic gas (specifically binary BECs) described by a nonlinear Schrödinger equation featuring a logarithmic nonlinearity arising from LHY corrections.

**Key observables.** Vortex core size, vortex energy, circulating current density, and particle number stability (dN/dmu).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Topological charge (l), chemical potential (mu), scale factor (alpha), and the LHY correction term.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The solution assumes a weak beyond-mean-field interaction to ensure series convergence and restricts the radial dependence of the beta/gamma ratio to a characteristic scale to maintain analytical consistency.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the stability of the solution via dN/dmu; Figure 2 shows radial density profiles for different topological charges; Figure 3 shows the evolution of the vortex core in the x-y plane; Figure 4 illustrates current density growth with increasing topological charge.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of LHY corrections in 2D, presents a mathematical ansatz and derivation using the Lambert W function, performs stability analysis via the Vakhitov-Kolokolov criterion, and concludes with a discussion of physical implications and limitations.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

In this investigation, we provide an exact analytical vortex solution for a Bose liquid in two dimensions with beyond mean-field correction (BMF). Analytical solutions in two-dimensional systems with BMF corrections are rarely found in the literature. The present result provides a clear framework for understanding vortex structures in low-dimensional quantum fluids and serves as a reliable benchmark for future theoretical and experimental studies.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.00246"></a>
### [Fidelity-Guaranteed Entanglement Routing with Distributed Purification Planning](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00246v1)

**Authors:** Anthony Gatti, Anoosha Fayyaz, Prashant Krishnamurthy, Kaushik P. Seshadreesan, Amy Babay  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.00246v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

**Summary.** The paper introduces Q-GUARD, a distributed routing algorithm for quantum networks that ensures end-to-end entanglement meets specific fidelity requirements. By using local link-state information and adaptive purification planning, it significantly improves the success rate and service range of quantum communication compared to traditional throughput-only methods.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Existing quantum network routing algorithms either ignore fidelity or require centralized global knowledge, leading to high latency and decoherence. There is a need for a distributed, fidelity-aware routing protocol that ensures end-to-end entanglement meets specific thresholds.

**Main result.** The Q-GUARD algorithm increases the qualified success rate from under 20% to over 85% on 4-hop paths and nearly doubles the effective service radius in Euclidean distance compared to throughput-only baselines.

**Method.** The authors propose Q-GUARD, an online distributed algorithm using a k-hop local link-state model, a Werner-state equal-split rule for fidelity target allocation, and an Expected Goodput (EXG) metric for path segment selection.

**Model / system.** A quantum network model consisting of nodes and links capable of entanglement generation, BBPSSW purification, and entanglement swapping, modeled using Werner states and a time-slotted, decentralized k-hop topology.

**Key observables.** Qualified throughput (eps), qualified success rate, service radius (Euclidean distance), and expected goodput (EXG).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Fidelity threshold (Fth), k-hop communication radius, path width (W), hardware heterogeneity (sigma_eta), and purification rounds (Rmax).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes the BBPSSW purification protocol, Werner-state noise model, and a time-slotted operation with limited k-hop local information exchange.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 compares the routing pipeline of Q-CAST vs Q-GUARD; Figure 2 shows a path detour example; Figure 3 shows performance metrics including qualified throughput vs fidelity threshold, throughput vs hardware heterogeneity, and throughput vs offered load.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of fidelity-aware routing, presents the Q-GUARD and Q-GUARD-WS algorithms, details the physical model and metrics (EXG), evaluates performance via large-scale simulations on synthetic topologies, and analyzes computational complexity.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Many quantum-network applications require end-to-end Bell pairs whose fidelity exceeds a request-specific threshold, but existing entanglement routing algorithms either optimize only throughput without regard for fidelity or enforce fidelity guarantees using centralized controllers with global link-state knowledge. We present Q-GUARD, an online entanglement routing algorithm that enforces per-request fidelity thresholds within a distributed protocol model in which nodes exchange link-state information only with their $k$-hop neighbors. After link outcomes are realized in each slot, Q-GUARD builds per-link purification cost tables from realized Bell pairs, allocates per-hop fidelity targets using a Werner-state equal-split rule, and selects between candidate path segments using a segment-local expected-goodput (EXG) metric that jointly accounts for swap success, purification overhead, and resource availability. We also introduce Q-GUARD-WS, an extension that exploits per-link hardware quality estimates to allocate purification effort non-uniformly across hops. On synthetic 100-node topologies with heterogeneous link fidelity and stochastic BBPSSW purification, Q-GUARD raises the qualified success rate from under 20\% to over 85\% on 4-hop paths and nearly doubles the qualified service radius in Euclidean distance relative to throughput-only and naive-purification baselines, while Q-GUARD-WS provides additional throughput gains under high hardware heterogeneity.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Incommensurate Magnetic Ordered Phase with Enhanced Low-Temperature Magnetic Specific Heat in SmAu$_3$Al$_7$](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00540v1)

**Authors:** Ryuji Higashinaka, Takuma Iwami, Kohsuke Saitou, Takashi U. Ito, Chihiro Tabata, Koji Kaneko, Takashi Ohhara, Ryoji Kiyanagi, Akiko Nakao, Jumpei G. Nakamura, Wataru Higemoto, Akihiro Koda, Shinsaku Kambe, Yuji Aoki, Tatsuma D. Matsuda

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that the magnetic order in SmAu3Al7 is a spatially homogeneous incommensurate state rather than a partially disordered one. It shows that the magnetic structure remains stable through the secondary transition at 0.9 K. The study also links the incommensurate order to an enhanced low-temperature electronic specific heat, potentially due to Kondo screening.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to elucidate the nature of the incommensurate magnetic ordered state in SmAu3Al7 and determine if the transition at T* = 0.9 K involves a change in magnetic structure or a partially disordered state.

**Main result.** The researchers confirmed a spatially homogeneous incommensurate magnetic order that remains structurally unchanged across the T* transition, ruling out previous proposals of a partially disordered state.

**Method.** The study employs neutron scattering (TOF and triple-axis spectrometry) and muon spin rotation (muSR) to probe magnetic structures and local fields, alongside magnetometry and specific heat measurements.

**Model / system.** The physical system is SmAu3Al7, a single-crystal Sm-based strongly correlated electron system with an ScRh3Si7-type structure where Sm ions are in cages.

**Key observables.** Incommensurate propagation vector q, magnetic Bragg peak intensity, spontaneous internal magnetic fields (Bmax), magnetic susceptibility, and electronic specific heat coefficient (gamma).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Transition temperatures TN = 2.8 K and T* = 0.9 K; propagation vector q = (0.30, 0, 1.33); electronic specific heat coefficient gamma > 1 J/(mol K^2).

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows crystal structure, phase diagram, and thermodynamic properties; Fig 2-4 present neutron diffraction patterns and 1D/2D scans of magnetic Bragg peaks; Fig 5 shows muon spin rotation spectra and the scaling of the local field with neutron intensity.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the material and its phase transitions, presents neutron diffraction data to identify the incommensurate structure, uses muSR to investigate local field homogeneity and the T* transition, and concludes by discussing the connection between magnetic order and specific heat enhancement.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Neutron scattering and muon spin rotation ($μ$SR) measurements on single-crystal SmAu$_3$Al$_7$ reveal magnetically ordered states associated with successive transitions at $T_{\rm N}$ = 2.8 K and $T^*$ = 0.9 K. Magnetic Bragg peaks appear below $T_{\rm N}$ with an incommensurate (IC) propagation vector ${\bf q}$ = (0.30, 0, 1.33). $μ$SR detects spontaneous internal fields below $T_{\rm N}$, and the spectral shape is consistent with the IC magnetic ordering. No anomalies are observed at $T^*$, indicating that the magnetic structure remains essentially unchanged below and above $T^*$. The magnetic order is revealed to be a spatially homogeneous long-range ordered state, rather than a partially disordered state proposed in earlier studies. The possible connection between the IC magnetic order and the enhanced low-temperature magnetic specific heat is discussed.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.00274"></a>
### [Low-temperature Depletion of Superfluid Density in the Absence of Galilean Symmetry](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00274v1)

**Authors:** Viktor Berger, Nikolay Prokof'ev, Boris Svistunov  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum gases · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.00274v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `analog quantum simulation` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00274_figures/2605.00274_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. Superfluid stiffness depletion as a function of x = (cT)3 for soft-core bosons in 2D with U/t = 4 (for system size L × L = 64 × 64). Solid (black) curve is the f(x) = A + Bx theoretical prediction with A = ns(T = t/6) and B fixed by the measured system parameters according to Eq. (3).</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00274_figures/2605.00274_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> FIG. 2. Period of torsional oscillator as a function of T 4</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper develops a new theory for how superfluid density decreases at low temperatures in systems where Galilean invariance is broken. It moves beyond the standard Landau theory by accounting for anharmonic terms in the hydrodynamic action. The results show a universal scaling law that links temperature-dependent properties to finite-size effects.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a universal framework for understanding how broken symmetries affect the thermodynamics of many-body systems, specifically relating temperature-driven depletion to finite-size effects in quantum fluids.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Generalizing the theory of low-temperature superfluid density depletion to systems where Galilean invariance is broken by factors like disorder, lattice potentials, or porous media.

**Main result.** The authors derive a general formula for the temperature dependence of superfluid stiffness that includes anharmonic terms, revealing a universal scaling of T^{d+1} and a connection between finite-temperature and finite-size effects.

**Method.** The study uses Popov's hydrodynamic action with anharmonic terms for theoretical derivation and validates the results using Worm algorithm Path-Integral Monte Carlo simulations of interacting lattice bosons.

**Model / system.** The theory applies to superfluids lacking Galilean symmetry, such as ultracold atoms in optical lattices, supersolids, and Helium-4 in porous media, modeled via an anharmonic hydrodynamic Hamiltonian.

**Key observables.** Superfluid stiffness/density depletion (delta n_s), sound velocity, compressibility, and density fluctuations.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Low-temperature regime, dimensionality (d), sound velocity (c), compressibility (kappa), and parameters nu, gamma, and sigma representing anharmonicity.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The theory assumes the low-temperature asymptotic regime where only the acoustic part of the phonon branch is excited.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the failure of Landau theory in non-Galilean systems, presents a new hydrodynamic theory based on Popov's action, provides numerical validation using PIMC simulations, and discusses universal scaling and experimental implications.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Landau theory of superfluidity associates low-temperature flow of the normal component with the phonon wind. This picture does not apply to superfluids in which Galilean invariance is broken either by disorder, porous media, or lattice potential, and the phonon wind is no longer solely responsible for depletion of the superfluid component. Based on Popov's hydrodynamic action with anharmonic terms, we present a general theory for low-temperature ($T$) dependence of the superfluid stiffness, which reproduces Landau result as a special case when several parameters of the hydrodynamic action are fixed by Galilean invariance, and validate it with numerical simulations of interacting lattice bosons. In a broader context, our approach reveals universal low-temperature thermodynamics of superfluids with an intrinsic connection between finite-$T$ and finite-size ($L$) effects implying universal scaling, $T^{d+1}$ and $1/L^{d+1}$, respectively, for a large class of thermodynamic quantities. We discuss the experimental detection of this law, and compare our prediction to the existing literature.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Parity-dependent reentrant topology in a Su--Schrieffer--Heeger chain with power-law quasiperiodic modulation](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00543v1)

**Summary.** This paper explores how the parity of a power-law exponent in quasiperiodic modulation controls the emergence of reentrant topological phases in an SSH chain. It reveals that odd and even exponents lead to fundamentally different topological phase diagrams. The findings are supported by both analytical theory and a proposed implementation using topoelectrical circuits.

**Why it may be interesting.** The discovery of a parity-dependent mechanism for inducing topological Anderson-like phases in quasiperiodic systems provides new insights into how controlled disorder can be used to engineer topological states.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how power-law quasiperiodic modulation in a 1D SSH chain induces reentrant topological transitions and how the parity of the modulation exponent affects the phase diagram.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that the topological phase structure is parity-dependent: odd-power modulations can induce reentrant topology from the clean trivial regime, whereas even-power modulations only allow reentrance from the negative trivial regime. This effect is robust across the interpolation between smooth and square-wave-like modulation profiles.

**Method.** The study combines analytical calculations of the zero-mode inverse localization length with numerical evaluations of a real-space topological indicator and proposes an electrical-circuit implementation for experimental verification.

**Model / system.** A one-dimensional Su-Schrieffer-Heeger (SSH) chain with power-law quasiperiodically modulated intracell hopping, which can be realized via an AC electrical-circuit chain.

**Key observables.** Zero-mode inverse localization length (nu), real-space topological indicator (Q), node-voltage distribution, and two-point impedance.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Modulation strength (lambda), modulation exponent (n), modulation profile parameter (beta), and clean-limit intracell hopping (t1).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study assumes the thermodynamic limit for analytical calculations and focuses on the chiral-symmetric regime.

**Figures summary.** Figure 5 shows phase diagrams in the (beta, lambda) plane for odd and even n; Figure 6 provides a schematic of the electrical-circuit implementation.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the modulated SSH model, presents analytical derivations for phase boundaries in specific limits, provides numerical phase diagrams and parity-dependent analysis, and concludes with a proposed experimental circuit implementation.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate reentrant topological transitions in a one-dimensional Su--Schrieffer--Heeger chain with power-law quasiperiodically modulated intracell hopping. The modulation is characterized by a positive integer exponent $n$ and a tunable parameter $β$, which continuously interpolates between the smooth power-law quasiperiodic limit and a sign-function limit that becomes square-wave-like for odd $n$ and uniform for even $n$. By combining analytical calculations of the zero-mode inverse localization length with numerical evaluations of a real-space topological indicator, we determine the topological phase diagrams in the $β\to 0$, $β\to\infty$, and finite-$β$ regimes. We show that deterministic quasiperiodic modulation can induce TAI-like reentrant topological phases within finite parameter windows. The formation of these phases depends crucially on the parity of $n$: for positive modulation strength, odd-power modulations can induce reentrant topology from the clean trivial regime $|t_1|>1$, whereas even-power modulations allow such reentrance only from the negative clean trivial regime $t_1<-1$. Exact analytical expressions for the zero-mode inverse localization length are obtained for $n=1,2,3,4$, yielding explicit or implicit transition conditions. The finite-$β$ results demonstrate that the parity-dependent structure remains robust throughout the interpolation between the two limiting cases. This parity effect originates from whether the modulation preserves or removes the sign structure of $\cos x$. We further propose an electrical-circuit implementation and discuss experimentally accessible signatures of the reentrant trivial--topological--trivial transition.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.00552"></a>
### [Suppression of Universal Errors in DFS-Encoded Superconducting Geometric Logical \emph{T} Gate](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00552v1)

**Authors:** Cheng-Yun Ding, Li-Hua Zhang, Jian Zhou  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.00552v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **2/5**

**Summary.** The paper proposes a new method for creating robust logical T-gates in superconducting processors using a combination of geometric pulses and decoherence-free encoding. By optimizing the trajectory of the quantum gate, the researchers achieved high-order suppression of the most common errors in transmon systems. This approach offers a more resource-efficient alternative to traditional magic state distillation for fault-tolerant quantum computing.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to open quantum systems and quantum optics researchers because it demonstrates advanced control techniques (composite pulses and geometric phases) to protect a logical qubit from specific environmental noise channels (collective dephasing and frequency drifts).

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The difficulty of implementing high-fidelity, non-Clifford T-gates in superconducting circuits due to universal errors like Rabi frequency fluctuations, detuning, and residual ZZ crosstalk, as well as the high resource overhead of magic state distillation.

**Main result.** The proposed optimized multi-loop composite geometric T-gate (OCGT) achieves fourth-order suppression of Rabi, detuning, and crosstalk errors while providing inherent immunity to collective dephasing.

**Method.** The authors use nonadiabatic geometric quantum computing (NGQC) combined with a multi-loop optimized composite pulse engineering strategy and Decoherence-Free Subspace (DFS) encoding.

**Model / system.** A system of two capacitively coupled transmon qubits in a 2D square lattice, utilizing a parametrically tunable coupling model via ac microwave driving and DFS encoding in the single-excitation subspace.

**Key observables.** Gate fidelity, logical-state populations, and leakage error rates.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Rabi frequency error (epsilon), detuning error (delta), residual ZZ crosstalk (eta), and microwave modulation parameters (beta and Delta).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The model assumes a two-excitation subspace and focuses on specific noise channels, with potential leakage errors from high-frequency terms addressed via parameter optimization.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the physical transmon setup; Fig 2 illustrates the evolution trajectories for single-loop, conventional, and optimized multi-loop strategies; Fig 3 compares gate robustness against Rabi errors; Fig 6 shows fidelity landscapes and time-resolved dynamics; Fig 11 plots fidelity as a function of path parameters.

**Paper structure.** The paper identifies the T-gate bottleneck, proposes a hardware-efficient geometric approach using DFS, details the tunable transmon Hamiltonian, introduces a unified framework for gate construction, compares three trajectory strategies, and demonstrates numerical superiority in error suppression and leakage mitigation.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

High-fidelity logical \emph{T}-gate realization constitutes a core prerequisite for large-scale fault-tolerant quantum computing. However, conventional magic state distillation requires massive physical qubit overhead across successive distillation rounds, alongside sophisticated measurement and feedback control, thereby inducing considerable spatial and temporal resource consumption. Herein, we propose a controlled superconducting geometric logical \emph{T} gate scheme that achieves high-order suppression of universal errors, by integrating decoherence-free subspace encoding with multi-loop optimized composite geometric pulse engineering. Guided by tailored trajectory design, we systematically establish unified gate construction frameworks for conventional geometric, composite geometric, and optimized composite geometric protocols. By flexibly controling additional parametric degrees of freedom, the proposed scheme achieves substantially enhanced robustness against diverse noise sources. Numerical simulations reveal that, within tunable superconducting quantum circuits, our geometric logical \emph{T} gate outperforms both conventional composite geometric and dynamical gates in suppressing Rabi frequency, detuning, and residual inter-qubit crosstalk errors that can all be suppressed to the fourth order, while additionally providing inherent suppression of collective dephasing errors. The present strategy alleviates intrinsic limitations of mainstream approaches and opens a promising avenue toward robust high-fidelity logical \emph{T} gate construction.

</details>

### [Cryogenic Graphene-Based Phase Modulators for Quantum Information Processing](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00112v1)

**Authors:** Leonard Barboza Navarro, Maria Carolina Volpato, Alisson Ronieri Cadore, Pierre-Louis de Assis

**Summary.** The paper presents a theoretical optimization of graphene-based phase modulators for use in cryogenic quantum photonic circuits. It demonstrates that operating at 10 K significantly reduces device footprint and loss by leveraging the Pauli-blocking effect. The study identifies HfO2-based designs as a superior candidate for high-efficiency, low-loss modulation.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to quantum optics and photonic quantum computing as it provides design guidelines for the hardware components (modulators) necessary for controlling photonic qubits in scalable, cryogenic-compatible integrated circuits.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Optimizing the performance of graphene-based electro-optic phase modulators for integrated photonic quantum information processing, specifically focusing on achieving low loss, high bandwidth, and compactness in cryogenic environments.

**Main result.** Cryogenic operation (10 K) enhances performance by sharpening the Fermi-Dirac distribution, enabling near-pure phase modulation with insertion losses below 0.3 dB and modulation lengths below 50 um.

**Method.** A combination of electromagnetic simulations using COMSOL Multiphysics and a physically consistent description of graphene conductivity based on the Kubo formalism.

**Model / system.** A dual-layer graphene (DSLG) capacitor integrated on silicon nitride (Si3N4) ridge waveguides, utilizing electrostatic tuning of the Fermi level to achieve the Pauli-blocking transparency regime.

**Key observables.** Effective refractive index (real and imaginary parts), phase shift, modal power attenuation (MPA), insertion loss, modulation efficiency (Vpi L), and 3-dB bandwidth.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Temperature (10 K to 300 K), graphene mobility, Fermi level (eV), dielectric spacer thickness and permittivity (HfO2 vs Al2O3), and waveguide dimensions.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Graphene is modeled as an infinitesimally thin conductive sheet with a fixed Fermi velocity; materials are assumed to be linear, isotropic, and non-magnetic.

**Figures summary.** Schematics of the DSLG architecture, cross-sectional views of the waveguide, effective index plots for TE/TM modes, frequency response comparisons, and equivalent electrical circuit diagrams.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the need for cryogenic modulators, describes the DSLG-Si3N4 platform and Kubo-based conductivity model, presents parametric studies on geometry and materials, analyzes temperature and mobility impacts, and concludes with performance comparisons to state-of-the-art technologies.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Electro-optic modulators are key components for photonic quantum computing, particularly in fully cryovenic integrated platforms where low loss and compactness are critical. We present a systematic theoretical investigation of compact dual-layer graphene (DSLG) electro-optic phase modulators integrated on silicon nitride waveguides, with emphasis on cryogenic operation. By combining electromagnetic simulations with a physically consistent description of graphene conductivity based on the Kybo formalism, we analyze the interplay between electrostatic tuning, optical mode confinement, and material-dependent losses. We show that cryogenic operation enhances device performance by sharpening the Fermi-Dirac distribution, enabling access to the Pauli-blocking regime at lower Fermi levels and reducing the required modulation length. Through optimization of the waveguide geometry, dielectric spacer thickness and permittivity, and graphene quality, we identify regimes that simultaneously minimize insertion loss and device footprint under realistic voltage constraints. The optimized designs achieve near-pure phase modulation with insertion losses below 0.3 dB and modulation lengths below 50 um at 10 K, while maintaining GHz-scale bandwidths. These results provide quantitative design guidelines for low-loss, compact, cryogenic graphene phase modulators for scalable integrated quantum photonics.

</details>

### [Data-Driven Modelling to predict forest fire spread in the Patagonian region in Argentina](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00167v1)

**Authors:** Lucas Becerra, Monica Malen Denham, Alejandro B. Kolton, Karina Laneri  
**Type:** both · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.00167v1>

**Summary.** This paper presents a hybrid computational method that uses machine learning to optimize a physics-based reaction-diffusion-convection model for predicting forest fires. By combining Genetic Algorithms with XGBoost, the researchers can efficiently estimate difficult-to-measure environmental parameters in complex, heterogeneous landscapes.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The difficulty of accurately predicting wildfire spread in Patagonia due to the lack of local parameters and the inadequacy of global models for heterogeneous landscapes.

**Main result.** A hybrid framework combining a Genetic Algorithm with XGBoost successfully recovers model parameters, with XGBoost significantly improving accuracy in low-dimensional scenarios.

**Method.** A hybrid approach using a Genetic Algorithm (GA) for global parameter search and an XGBoost surrogate model for fine-tuning and accelerating the optimization process.

**Model / system.** A Reaction-Diffusion-Convection (RDC) model, phenomenologically inspired by the SIR epidemic framework, applied to wildfire propagation across a heterogeneous landscape of different vegetation types.

**Key observables.** Spatial overlap between simulated and reference burned areas, fire front velocity, and fire front width.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Ignition rate (beta), extinction rate (gamma), diffusion constant (D), advection weights (A and B for wind and slope), and ignition coordinates (x, y).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The diffusion constant is assumed to be homogeneous; the wind field is assumed to be stationary; the model does not capture microscopic combustion physics.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1-3 show reference wildfire patterns for different experimental complexities; Figure 4 shows fitness evolution; Figure 5 shows a fitness valley in the beta-gamma landscape; Figure 6 shows parameter correlation; Figure 7 shows convergence of ignition coordinates; Figure 8 shows spatial difference maps.

**Paper structure.** Introduction to the wildfire problem, description of the RDC model and numerical implementation, presentation of the GA and XGBoost optimization framework, results from three experiments of increasing complexity, and discussion of limitations and future work.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Wildfires are among the most severe disturbances affecting forest ecosystems, with over 50,000 hectares burned in Patagonia, Argentina, during 2025 alone. This study implements a Reaction-Diffusion-Convection (RDC) model to simulate wildfire spread in the Steffen and Martin Lakes area, a region severely impacted by fires. By integrating high-resolution maps of slope, wind velocity, and vegetation, we conducted three computational experiments of increasing complexity to simulate fire propagation across heterogeneous landscapes.   We employed a Genetic Algorithm (GA) to recover reference model parameters by maximizing the spatial overlap between simulated and reference burned areas. Subsequently, parameter estimates were refined using XGBoost to improve accuracy. Results demonstrate that the GA accurately recovers reference parameters across all scenarios, while the XGBoost fine-tuning significantly enhances accuracy in simpler cases. This integrated framework offers a systematic approach for estimating difficult-to-measure wildfire parameters, demonstrating the potential of hybrid computational methods for wildfire modeling and forest management.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.00107"></a>
### [Efficient Mutation Testing of Quantum Machine Learning Models](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00107v1)

**Authors:** Emma Andrews, Prabhat Mishra  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.00107v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00107_figures/2605.00107_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Fig. 1: An overview of mutation testing.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00107_figures/2605.00107_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Fig. 2: Example of mutation operations. The CNOT gate between qubits q1 and q2 is removed with a remove gate operation. A new one-qubit gate is added with the add gate operation after the first Hadamard gate on qubit q4. The Pauli-X gate acting on qubit q2 is changed to a semantically equivalent one-qubit gate on the same qubit and in the same location with the change gate operation. The measurement of q2 is removed with the remove measurement operation. A new measurement is added at the end of the circuit on qubit q4 with the add measurement operation. Note that typically one mutation is performed to generate the mutant.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00107_figures/2605.00107_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> Fig. 5: Typical structure of a quantum convolutional neural network (QCNN). It consists of a convolutional layer followed by a pooling layer. The next round of these pairs of layers reduces the number of qubits it operates over.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00107_figures/2605.00107_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> Fig. 3: Components of a quantum neural network. The data is first embedded into the quantum circuit using a feature mapping. With the embedded data, the ansatz can process it, learning features and other relationships depending on the model objective. Finally, measurement of the quantum circuit produces the result of the model, such as a class label in classification tasks.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00107_figures/2605.00107_fig5.jpg" alt="Figure 5">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 5.</b> Fig. 4: Example of two-qubit ZZ feature map. x[0] and x[1] are the values taken from the input data sample.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper introduces an efficient mutation testing framework specifically designed for Quantum Machine Learning models. By targeting the unique structural components of QNNs and QCNNs, the method reduces computational overhead and improves the detection of faults in quantum circuits.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Existing mutation testing for quantum circuits is too generalized and inefficient for Quantum Machine Learning (QML) models, leading to excessive redundant mutants and failure to detect application-specific faults.

**Main result.** The authors developed a directed mutation framework that significantly reduces mutant redundancy (up to 9.5x) while achieving higher mutation scores, particularly through targeted gate and layer modifications.

**Method.** The paper proposes seven new mutation operations (e.g., Ansatz Parameter Change, Layer Shuffling, and Gate Substitution) and a directed generation technique to target the structural components of QNNs and QCNNs.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on Quantum Neural Networks (QNNs) and Quantum Convolutional Neural Networks (QCNNs) using various feature maps (ZFM, ZZFM, Amplitude Embedding) and ansätze (RealAmplitudes, EfficientSU2).

**Key observables.** Mutation Score (ratio of killed to total mutants), Survival Rate, and Generation/Evaluation time.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Rotation gate parameters, input data features, and circuit depth (number of layers).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes that breaking entanglement and symmetry in feature maps is a primary way to induce detectable faults; assumes the use of classical simulators (Qiskit-Aer) for evaluation.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the mutation workflow; Figure 2 illustrates standard mutation operations; Figure 3 and 4 detail QNN/feature map structures; Figure 5 shows QCNN architecture; Figure 7 presents the seven new mutation operations; Figure 8 uses t-SNE to visualize decision boundary changes.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of QML verification, defines the structural components of QNNs, proposes seven new mutation operations, describes the experimental setup using various datasets and ansätze, and evaluates the efficiency and effectiveness of the new approach against existing methods.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum machine learning integrates the strengths of quantum computing and machine learning, enabling models to learn complex features using fewer parameters than their classical counterparts. Due to the increasing complexity of quantum machine learning models, it is necessary to verify that the implementation of these models satisfy the design specification and be free of bugs and faults. Mutation testing is a promising avenue to identify faulty quantum circuits that do not meet design specifications or contain defects by intentionally inserting faults into the quantum circuit. It is necessary to define mutation operations to inject faults into quantum circuits to ensure that a test suite is robust enough to evaluate an implementation against its design specification. In this paper, we extend mutation testing to quantum machine learning applications, primarily quantum neural network models. Specifically, this paper makes two important contributions. We define new mutation operations for efficient fault insertion compared to state-of-the-art approaches. We also present a directed mutation generation technique to reduce redundant mutant circuits. Extensive experimental evaluation demonstrates that our approach generates a more diverse and representative set of mutants, effectively addressing faults that traditional techniques fail to expose.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Electrical detection of spin-flip transition in metal/Na5Co15.5Te6O36 heterostructure](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00491v1)

**Authors:** Hirotsugu Tagami, Takuya Kawada, Yuki Shiomi  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.00491v1>

**Summary.** The researchers investigated the origin of magnetoresistance anomalies in Pt/NCTO and Cu/NCTO heterostructures during magnetic spin-flip transitions. They found that the primary cause of the resistance jumps is strain induced by magnetostriction, though interface spin accumulation contributes to the high-field behavior. This work provides a method for the electrical detection of magnetic transitions in Ising-type antiferromagnets.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Determining whether magnetoresistance (MR) anomalies at spin-flip transitions in metal/magnetic insulator heterostructures are driven by interface spin accumulation or by magnetostriction-induced strain.

**Main result.** The MR anomalies at the spin-flip transition are attributed to magnetostriction-induced strain, while the high-field MR behavior is driven by the interaction between interface spin accumulation and induced magnetization.

**Method.** Longitudinal MR measurements and SQUID magnetometry were performed on Pt and Cu thin films deposited on Na5Co15.5Te6O36 (NCTO) single crystals.

**Model / system.** The system consists of Pt and Cu thin films (8.3 nm) on a single-crystal Ising-type antiferromagnetic insulator, Na5Co15.5Te6O36 (NCTO), which features Co ion sublattices and undergoes transitions to partially disordered antiferromagnetic states.

**Key observables.** Longitudinal magnetoresistance (MR), magnetization (M), magnetic susceptibility (chi), and magnetostriction coefficient (lambda).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Neel temperature (TN approx 50 K), transition to frozen PDA state (approx 20 K), and magnetic field strength (up to 9 T).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study assumes that the similarity in MR jumps between Pt and Cu (which have different spin-orbit interactions) implies a strain-based mechanism for the spin-flip transition anomalies.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the crystal structure of NCTO, including the Co(1) triangular networks and zigzag patterns.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the heterostructure and the scientific problem, describes the experimental setup and material properties, presents MR and magnetization results, analyzes the physical mechanisms (strain vs. spin-current), and concludes with implications for antiferromagnetic spintronics.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We report on the longitudinal magnetoresistance (MR) in thin metal films on an Ising-type antiferromagnetic insulator, Na5Co15.5Te6O36 (NCTO). Steep changes in the MR spectra with hysteresis were observed at spin-flip transitions driven by magnetic fields applied along the easy axis of the NCTO crystal. The MR jumps almost follow step-like changes in magnetization at the spin-flip transition. At very low temperatures where Co moments are partially frozen, the MR anomalies exhibit a tunnel-magnetoresistance-like shape. The observed MR anomalies at the spin-flip transition are attributed to strain effects via magnetostriction upon the magnetic-structure change of the Co nets in NCTO, because similar MR jumps are observed in both Pt/NCTO and Cu/NCTO. Interestingly, we found that the high-field slopes of the MR spectra show opposite signs between Pt/NCTO and Cu/NCTO at low temperatures. Because the opposite signs of the high-field MR are prominent below the antiferromagnetic transition temperature of NCTO, the interaction between the interface spin accumulation and magnetization is likely to contribute to the MR effect in the induced ferromagnetic state.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.00629"></a>

The pair correlation function was computed using the Aziz I pair potential, whereas static structure factor was computed using both the Aziz I and Lennard-Jones potentials.</figcaption>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00629_figures/2605.00629_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">

</div>

**Summary.** This paper presents a large-scale Quantum Monte Carlo study of superfluid 4He using 2,048 atoms to achieve much higher precision than previous studies. It provides updated values for the ground-state condensate fraction and energy, effectively benchmarking modern simulation techniques against experimental data.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to provide updated, high-precision numerical estimates for the superfluid phase of 4He, addressing inaccuracies and small system sizes in legacy simulations.

**Main result.** The paper provides a revised ground-state condensate fraction of 0.0715 +/- 0.0006, which is lower than previous QMC estimates but consistent with experiment, and establishes a new benchmark for energetic and structural properties.

**Method.** State-of-the-art Quantum Monte Carlo (QMC) simulations using the canonical version of the continuous-space Worm Algorithm (WA).

**Model / system.** A system of 2,048 identical, spin-zero, point-like helium-4 atoms at saturated vapor pressure, modeled using the Aziz I and Aziz II interatomic potentials.

**Key observables.** Kinetic and potential energy, superfluid fraction, condensate fraction, pair correlation function, static structure factor, and one-particle density matrix.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Temperature range (0.25 K to 2 K), fixed equilibrium density, and imaginary time step (tau) for extrapolation.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Uses the Born-Oppenheimer approximation and treats the atoms as non-relativistic particles with pairwise-decomposed potential energy.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows time-step extrapolation for kinetic energy; Figure 2 compares the one-particle density matrix at different time steps; Figure 3 compares structural properties across different potentials; Figure 4 and 5 illustrate the behavior of the one-body density matrix near the transition temperature.

**Paper structure.** The paper identifies limitations in previous studies, describes the Worm Algorithm methodology and time-step convergence, presents updated energetic and structural results, analyzes the temperature dependence of the condensate fraction, and concludes with a comparison of different interatomic potentials.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Detailed numerical results obtained with state-of-the-art Quantum Monte Carlo (QMC) simulations are presented for the superfluid phase of $^4$He at saturated vapor pressure. The aim of this contribution is that of providing reliable, up-to-date estimates for this archetypal superfluid, reflecting the methodological progress that has taken place over the past two decades. We simulate a system comprising 2,048 helium atoms, i.e., an order of magnitude greater in size than those for which results currently regarded as standard references were originally obtained. We offer revised estimates for energetic and structural properties, as well as for the ground state condensate fraction.

</details>

### [Quantized Collective Fluctuations in Correlated Fermion Systems](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00211v1)

**Authors:** S. S. Onuchin, Ya. S. Lyakhova, L. D. Silakov, A. N. Rubtsov  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.00211v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** The paper introduces the Q-FLF method, an extension of the Fluctuating Local Field approach that incorporates quantum Matsubara modes to better describe collective excitations. It demonstrates that while low-frequency modes are sufficient for global thermodynamic quantities, higher-frequency modes are necessary for accurate single-particle spectra. This provides a selective tool for studying the impact of specific fluctuation channels in correlated systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing an efficient theoretical approach to quantify the specific contributions of individual bosonic Matsubara modes (quantum fluctuations) to the observable properties of correlated fermion systems.

**Main result.** The proposed Q-FLF method shows that low-frequency bosonic modes quantitatively impact global observables like total energy and susceptibility, whereas an accurate description of single-particle Green's functions requires higher-frequency modes.

**Method.** The Quantum Fluctuating Local Field (Q-FLF) method, which extends the classical FLF approach by incorporating selected bosonic Matsubara modes into a Gaussian ensemble of fluctuating external fields.

**Model / system.** A one-dimensional (1D) Hubbard chain at half-filling with hopping constant t and on-site Coulomb interaction U, studied in finite sizes (N = 4, 6, 8, 10, 12).

**Key observables.** Matsubara Green's function, total energy per site, and antiferromagnetic susceptibility (specifically the Curie constant).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Interaction strength (U/t = 1), inverse temperature (beta), and system size (N).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The bosonic spectrum is truncated to include only the zeroth and +/- 1 Matsubara modes; the system is modeled using a minimal approximation of small-size chains to neglect spatial inhomogeneities.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the dispersion law and Fermi level for N=4 and N=6; Figure 2 compares the Green's function for Q-FLF, FLF0, and ED; Figure 3 compares total energy per site; Figure 4 shows the temperature dependence of the Curie constant.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the Q-FLF method, applies it to the 1D Hubbard model, evaluates its performance on global and single-particle observables, provides a diagrammatic interpretation of the expansion, and compares results against exact numerical benchmarks.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Collective excitations in fermionic systems play a crucial role in determining their physical properties. An important challenge is to develop efficient theoretical approaches for describing these excitations and their coupling to fermionic degrees of freedom. In this work, we revisit the problem of quantifying the contributions of individual bosonic modes of collective fluctuations to observable properties of correlated fermion systems within the framework of the Fluctuating Local Field (FLF) method. Whereas the auxiliary field in this method was previously considered only classically, we formulate its systematic extension termed Quantum FLF (Q-FLF) that incorporates selected bosonic Matsubara modes, thus tailoring it to description of quantum collective fluctuations. As a testbed, we apply the approach to a half-filled one-dimensional Hubbard chain and compute the Green's function, the total energy, and the antiferromagnetic susceptibility. Our results demonstrate that the proposed scheme enables an efficient and selective characterization of the contributions of individual bosonic modes. In particular, low Matsubara frequencies are found to have a quantitative impact on integrated observables such as total energy and antiferromagnetic susceptibility. At the same time, an accurate description of single-particle properties requires inclusion of higher-frequency bosonic modes.

</details>

### [Quantum Data Loading for Carleman Linearized Systems: Application to the Lattice-Boltzmann Equation](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00302v1)

**Authors:** Reuben Demirdjian, Thomas Hogancamp, Abeynaya Gnanasekaran, Amit Surana, Daniel Gunlycke  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.00302v1>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00302_figures/2605.00302_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">

</div>

**Summary.** This paper presents a new method for loading nonlinear fluid dynamics data onto a quantum computer by using Carleman linearization and a novel unitary embedding technique. It achieves polylogarithmic scaling with respect to the simulation grid size, making it a significant step toward efficient quantum simulation of complex classical fluids.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing an efficient quantum data loading strategy for simulating nonlinear dynamical systems, specifically addressing the high cost of decomposing Carleman-linearized matrices into a Linear Combination of Unitaries (LCU).

**Main result.** The authors introduce an LCNU-to-LCU strategy that embeds non-unitary terms into unitaries without increasing the number of terms, providing a framework that scales polylogarithmically with spatial and temporal discretization points for the 3D Lattice-Boltzmann Equation.

**Method.** The method uses Carleman linearization to transform nonlinear PDEs into linear systems, a 'judicious zero padding' technique to handle non-uniform submatrix sizes, and a unitary completion strategy to implement the resulting operators via quantum circuits.

**Model / system.** The primary application is the 3-dimensional Lattice-Boltzmann Equation (LBE), a discretized model for fluid dynamics involving streaming and collision operators (linear, quadratic, and cubic) on a periodic domain.

**Key observables.** T gate cost, number of LCU terms (Ns), condition number scaling, and Reynolds/Mach numbers.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Carleman truncation order (alpha), number of discrete velocities (Q), spatial grid points (n), and the Reynolds and Mach numbers.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The number of discrete velocities is a power of two; the density is approximately one; and the accuracy of the simulation depends on the truncation order alpha.

**Figures summary.** Figure 4.1 and 4.2 show quantum circuit embeddings for linearized terms; Figure 4.3 shows the most expensive circuit for the linear collision term; Table 1 summarizes resource costs for different lattice types (D1Q3, D2Q9, D3Q15).

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the LCNU-to-LCU strategy, develops a generalized framework for Carleman linearized systems, applies this to the 3D LBE, provides explicit circuit constructions and resource estimations, and concludes with a condition number stability analysis.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Herein, we introduce a strategy to decompose an arbitrary square matrix into a linear combination of non-unitaries (LCNU) where each non-unitary term is embedded into a unitary matrix. The result is a linear combination of unitaries (LCU) with an equal number of terms as the LCNU. Using this approach, we construct a generalized LCU framework for any Carleman linearized autonomous dynamical system with a polynomial nonlinearity. This framework is then used to construct an LCU for the 3-dimensional Carleman linearized lattice Boltzmann equation (LBE) in which the number of terms scales like $N_s \sim \mathcal{O}(α^2 Q^2)$, where $α$ is the Carleman truncation order and $Q$ is the number of discrete velocities from the LBE. Importantly, $N_s$ is completely independent of both the number of temporal and spatial discretization points. Lastly, we provide an estimate of our LCNU strategy's T gate cost in conjunction with (1) PREP and SELECT block encoding oracles, and (2) the variational quantum linear solver. In the former, the T cost scales like $\mathcal{O}(α^3 Q^2 (\log_2 n)^2)$, where $n$ is the total number of spatial grid points across all dimensions. Next, the latter requires $N_s^2(\log_2 (2n_tn^α)+1)$ circuits per iteration, with a worst case T gate cost of $\mathcal{O}(α(\log_2 Qn)^2)$ among them. We, therefore, provide an efficient decomposition strategy useful for both fault-tolerant and variational approaches.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.00312"></a>
### [Quantum Decoding Algorithms: Quantum Speedups in Optimization](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00312v1)

**Authors:** Jan Ljubas, Tim Byrnes  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.00312v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

The 2D visualization of the codewords was done with the help of t-SNE embedding.</figcaption>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">

<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> Figure 3. Approaches for tackling the OPI optimization problem. Shown are the best-known classical methods (left) and the quantum DQI approach (right). Using DQI, a higher constraint satisfaction rate can be attained using quantum interference and classical decoding methods.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00312_figures/2605.00312_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">

The circuit is marked with seven state snapshots, which correspond to the seven steps of the DQI algorithm given in Sec. 5.3. The circuit uses three registers referred to as the “weight” (W), “error” (E), and “syndrome” (S) registers. After the uncomputation in the W and E registers, qubits are returned back to the |0⟩state. The DQI state (42) prepared on the S register after step 7.</figcaption>

<img src="2605.00312_figures/2605.00312_fig5.jpg" alt="Figure 5">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 5.</b> Figure 5. Performance comparison of DQI and Prange’s algorithm. The plot shows the expected constrain satisfaction rate ⟨s⟩versus the polynomial degree n, each normalized to the characteristic of the ﬁeld p. For n/p ≈0.1, we obtain the results highlighted in Fig. 3. The largest performance gap ∆is achieved for n/p ≈0.293, where ⟨sDQI⟩/p ≈0.854 and ⟨sPrange⟩/p ≈0.646. This plot is adapted from Ref.22.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper introduces and reviews the Decoded Quantum Interferometry (DQI) algorithm, which leverages classical coding theory to solve optimization problems. It demonstrates a potential superpolynomial quantum speedup for specific polynomial intersection problems, offering a new paradigm for quantum advantage beyond standard Grover-like approaches.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Achieving a scalable quantum advantage in solving combinatorial optimization problems, specifically the max-LINSAT and Optimal Polynomial Intersection (OPI) classes.

**Main result.** The paper demonstrates that the Decoded Quantum Interferometry (DQI) algorithm provides a superpolynomial quantum speedup for the OPI problem and achieves a higher expected constraint satisfaction rate compared to classical benchmarks like Prange's algorithm.

**Method.** The DQI algorithm uses a 7-step quantum circuit involving polynomial amplification, Dicke state preparation, and quantum Fourier transforms to transform optimization into a quantum decoding problem using coding theory.

**Model / system.** The framework utilizes finite fields (Galois fields) and linear codes (such as Reed-Solable and Reed-Muller) to model optimization constraints, specifically focusing on the max-LINSAT and OPI problem structures.

**Key observables.** Expected constraint satisfaction rate (s-bar), Hamming weight, and the performance gap between quantum and classical algorithms.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Field size (p), number of constraints (m), number of variables (n), polynomial degree (u), and the minimum distance (d) of the underlying codes.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The efficiency and orthonormality of the algorithm depend on the existence of efficient classical decoders and a sufficiently large minimum distance (d_perp) in the dual code.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 visualizes the algorithm's foundation; Figure 2 shows a t-SNE visualization of decoding; Figure 3 compares classical vs. quantum optimization approaches; Figure 4 details the 7-step DQI circuit; Figure 5 plots satisfaction rate vs. problem scale; Figure 6 illustrates a bivariate mOPI instance.

**Paper structure.** The paper provides a self-contained review covering the mathematical foundations of Galois fields and coding theory, defines the max-LINSAT and OPI optimization problems, details the step-by-step DQI algorithm architecture, and concludes with performance comparisons and extensions to multivariate and non-diagonal Hamiltonians.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Attaining a quantum speedup in solving practically useful optimization problems has been one of the holy grails in the field of quantum computing. While prior approaches have demonstrated speedups for certain structured problem classes, establishing a clear and scalable advantage on broadly useful practical optimization problems remains challenging. Recently, a new approach to solving the max-LINSAT class of optimization problems has emerged, called Decoded Quantum Interferometry (DQI). In DQI, a combination of techniques rooted in (classical) coding theory and interferometry are used to obtain the solution of max-LINSAT. In the special problem instance of the optimal polynomial intersection (OPI) problem, strong evidence exists to show that an superpolynomial speedup exists over the best classical methods in obtaining an approximate solution. In this review, we give a self-contained description of DQI and the necessary background to understand the algorithm. Specifically, we give the essentials of Galois fields, optimization problems such as max-LINSAT and OPI, and coding theory, followed by a step-by-step walkthrough of the quantum algorithm and its operating principle.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.00747"></a>
### [Quantum Interval Bound Propagation for Certified Training of Quantum Neural Networks](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00747v1)

**Authors:** Emma Andrews, Nahyeon Kim, Prabhat Mishra  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.00747v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00747_figures/2605.00747_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Fig. 1: Example of an MNIST [3] image of digit 2 being adversarially perturbed. While a human can still identify the digit 2, a quantum classifier will incorrectly predict it as 3.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00747_figures/2605.00747_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Fig. 2: Common structure of QML models.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00747_figures/2605.00747_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> Figure 2 provides an overview of the basic structures typically found in QML models. These are the feature map, the ansatz, and measurement. The feature map is responsible for embedding the data into the quantum circuit of the QML model. The ansatz carries out the QML model computations, consisting of parameterized and entangling gates. The param- eters of the parameterized gates are updated over the course</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00747_figures/2605.00747_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> Fig. 4: An example of interval bound through model layers.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.00747_figures/2605.00747_fig5.jpg" alt="Figure 5">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 5.</b> Fig. 5: Overview of QIBP. In each layer, the interval shifts due to the layer operations, adjusting the worst case bounds. Once the interval is propagated through all layers, it results in the logits zK, which are used to predict the class label. QIBP optimizes the interval logits so that the lower bound of the correct class logit is greater than the upper bounds of all incorrect class logits.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a new certified training routine called Quantum Interval Bound Propagation (QIBP) to make quantum neural networks robust against adversarial attacks. By propagating mathematical bounds through the quantum circuit, the method provides a formal guarantee that predictions will remain correct within a specified noise range. The study highlights that affine arithmetic is more effective than standard interval arithmetic for maintaining tight bounds in complex quantum models.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Variational Quantum Classifiers (VQCs) are vulnerable to adversarial attacks where small input perturbations cause incorrect predictions, and there is a lack of certified training methods to guarantee robustness in the quantum domain.

**Main result.** The authors propose Quantum Interval Bound Propagation (QIBP), demonstrating that using affine arithmetic provides tighter bounds and higher certified accuracy than interval arithmetic, and that margin loss improves decision boundary robustness.

**Method.** The method propagates upper and lower bounds of input intervals through the quantum circuit layers (feature map, ansatz, and measurement) using interval and affine arithmetic to establish worst-case logits.

**Model / system.** The study uses Variational Quantum Circuits (VQCs) consisting of $n$ qubits, featuring an amplitude or angle embedding feature map, parameterized rotation gates ($R_Y, R_X$), and entangling gates (CNOT).

**Key observables.** Logits (expectation values of the Pauli-Z operator), test accuracy, certified accuracy, and PGD (Projected Gradient Descent) accuracy.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Perturbation magnitude (epsilon), weighting hyperparameter (kappa), number of qubits, number of layers, and number of classes.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes model layers can be treated via interval or affine arithmetic (linearity/monotonicity of gates) and uses Chebyshev approximation for nonlinear transformations.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows an adversarial attack on MNIST; Figure 2 shows the QML architecture; Figure 3 shows a specific two-layer ansatz; Figure 4 illustrates interval propagation; Figure 5 shows the QIBP framework; Figure 6 plots accuracy trends vs. qubit count.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of adversarial vulnerability in QML, presents the QIBP framework using interval and affine arithmetic, evaluates the method using various loss functions and architectures on MNIST-like datasets, and analyzes the impact of model complexity and hyperparameters.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum machine learning is a promising field for efficiently learning features of a dataset to perform a specified task, such as classification. Interval bound propagation (IBP) is a popular certified training method in classical machine learning, where the lower and upper bounds are tracked throughout the model. These bounds are used during training to ensure that the model is certified to predict the correct label even under adversarial perturbations. While IBP is successful in classical domain, there are limited certified training efforts in quantum domain. In this paper, we present quantum interval bound propagation (QIBP) to establish a certified training routine for quantum machine learning, certifying the accuracy of models under adversarial perturbations. We implement QIBP using both interval and affine arithmetic to explore the tradeoffs between the two implementations in terms of accuracy and other design considerations. Extensive evaluation demonstrates that the resulting certified trained models have robust decision boundaries, guaranteed to predict the correct class for the samples within the trained adversarial robustness bounds.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.00794"></a>
### [Quantum Simulation of Differential-Algebraic Equations with Applications to Unsteady Stokes Flow](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00794v1)

**Authors:** Hsuan-Cheng Wu, Xiantao Li  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.00794v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
